PAGE 1 of 199
======================================================================
Design Manual for Roads and Bridges
Highway Structures & Bridges
Design
CD 377
Requirements for road restraint systems
(formerly TD 19/06)
Version 5.0.0
Summary
This document details the requirements for road restraint systems, namely permanent safety
barriers, vehicle parapets, vehicle/pedestrian parapets, terminals, transitions, crash cushions,
pedestrian restraint systems, removable barrier systems, temporary vehicle restraint systems,
legacy vehicle restraint systems, vehicle arrester beds, anti-glare systems and cattle grids.
National Variation
This document has associated National Application Annexes providing alternative or supplementary content to that
given in the core document, which is relevant to specific Overseeing Organisations. National Application Annexes
are adjoined at the end of this document.
Feedback and Enquiries
Users of this document are encouraged to raise any enquiries and/or provide feedback on the content and usage of
this document to the dedicated National Highways team. The online feedback form for all enquiries and feedback
can be accessed at: www.standardsforhighways.co.uk/feedback.
This is a controlled document.
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Foreword
Foreword
Publishing information
This document is published by National Highways.
This document supersedes all previous revisions of CD 377 [Ref 57.N] which are withdrawn.
This document provides requirements and advice used to develop the design outputs required to
deliver the works in accordance with CC 400 [Ref 47.N], CC 401 [Ref 46.N] and CC 402 [Ref 74.N].
Contractual and legal considerations
This document forms part of the works specification. It does not purport to include all the necessary
provisions of a contract. Users are responsible for applying all appropriate documents applicable to
their contract.
7

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Introduction
Introduction
Background
This document is an update to CD 377 [Ref 57.N] and reflects changes to the Overseeing
Organisations' requirements. It also takes account of updated and new EU standards.
This document gives requirements for road restraint systems and it, together with the associated
RRRAP [Ref 81.N] and RRRAP User Guide [Ref 31.N], assists those involved in determining where
road restraint systems are warranted, and the minimum required parameters.
Assumptions made in the preparation of this document
The assumptions made in GG 101 [Ref 40.N] apply to this document.
This document is written on the basis that road restraint systems will be supplied and constructed in
accordance with the Manual of Contract Documents for Highway Works - CC 400 [Ref 47.N], CC 401
[Ref 46.N] and CC 402 [Ref 74.N], and all other works will be designed and specified in accordance
with the Design Manual for Roads and Bridges (DMRB).
Mutual recognition
Where there is a requirement in this document for compliance with any part of a British Standard,
technical specification or quality mark, that requirement may be met by compliance with the Mutual
Recognition clause in GG 101 [Ref 40.N].
8
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Terms and definitions
Terms and definitions
Terms associated with risk
Term
Definition
'Acceptable' and 'so far as is
reasonably practicable' level of
risk
The Road Restraint Risk Assessment Process ( RRRAP [Ref
81.N]) that forms part of this document uses the term
'acceptable' to indicate where a level of risk which is 'so far as is
reasonably practicable' is achieved in respect of vehicle restraint
system provision. 1
Hazard
A source of potential harm, loss or failure.
Other Parties (referred to as
"Others" in the
RRRAP [Ref 81.N])
A group or collection of people in a public place, such as a
school, hospital or railway, that can be injured in numbers by an
errant vehicle or by a hazard that is hit by an errant vehicle; or a
high value asset or facility that can be adversely affected by an
impact from an errant vehicle or by a hazard that is hit by an
errant vehicle.
Secondary event
An incident that can arise as a result of an initial event. 2
Note 1: Depending on the situation, this can be where safety barrier is not warranted, or can be
achieved over a range of safety barrier lengths.
Note 2: For instance, if a lighting column is struck and falls onto a carriageway, the struck lighting
column has the potential to cause a secondary event, such as being hit by a vehicle and hence
creating a risk of a secondary incident occurring.
Other terms
Term
Definition
Accommodation bridge
A structure that serves residential properties or a piece of land
but which is not considered a public highway.
Adjacent arrester bed
An arrester bed which is constructed adjacent to the nearside of
the carriageway in a widened section of the highway.
Adjoining paved surface
The paved area on the traffic side of a parapet immediately
adjacent to the plinth or base of the parapet.
Anti-glare system
A system fitted to a road restraint system to reduce the amount of
light from oncoming vehicle headlights (e.g. on adjacent roads).
Bi-directional crash cushion
A crash cushion which has been successfully tested with test
approaches 1 to 5 inclusive, in accordance with BS EN 1317-1
[Ref 62.N] and BS EN 1317-3 [Ref 44.N].
Carriageway
The area of the paved width which is trafficked by road users
under normal operation. 1, 2, 3, 4
Cattle grid
A device designed to prevent the passage of animals but allow
the passage of certain types of wheeled traffic.
10
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

PAGE 12 of 199

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Terms and definitions
Other terms (continued)
Term
Definition
Crash cushion
An energy absorption device installed in front of one or more
hazards to reduce the severity of an impact.
Crash cushions are stand alone devices, and are not required to
be connected to any other RRS.
Deformable safety barrier
A safety barrier that when tested in accordance with BS EN
1317-1 [Ref 62.N] and BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N], deflects from
its pre-impact position, i.e. it has a dynamic deflection which is
not zero.
Directional crash cushion
A crash cushion which has been successfully tested with test
approaches 1 to 3 inclusive, and either test approach 4 or test
approach 5, in accordance with BS EN 1317-1 [Ref 62.N] and
BS EN 1317-3 [Ref 44.N].
Durability
Ability of a product to maintain its required performance over
time, under the influence of foreseeable actions, subject to
normal maintenance being undertaken.
Dwarf retaining wall
A retaining wall less than 1.0m in height, measured from the
adjacent surface.
Dynamic Deflection (D)
The maximum lateral dynamic displacement of any point of the
traffic face of the vehicle restraint system.
Emergency Crossing Rapid
Access Point (ECRAP)
A type of emergency crossing point that may be opened to
permit emergency vehicles to travel the wrong way into a tunnel
in the event of an incident.
Energy absorbing terminal
A terminal attached to a VRS which, in test approach 1 (i.e.
head-on centre - refer to DD ENV 1317-4 2002 [Ref 45.N] or PD
CEN/TS 1317-7 [Ref 60.N]), does not allow the most forward
point of the car to cross the vehicle exit line R, or which crosses
line R at a speed less than or equal to 11 kilometres per hour.
Expansion joint
A non standard safety barrier element which allows for the
expansion and contraction of a structure. 5
Flat
A surface with a gradient not exceeding 1 in 40. 6
Full containment safety barrier
A safety barrier which can demonstrate the full level of vehicle
containment for which it has been classified (in accordance with
BS EN 1317-5 [Ref 54.N])
Front face of parapet
The face or part of the parapet nearest to vehicular traffic.
Gap in safety barrier
The longitudinal distance between two lengths of VRS measured
from the most downstream surface of one terminal to the most
upstream surface of the subsequent terminal.
Heavy goods vehicle (HGV)
A vehicle with a gross combination mass of over 3500kg.
11
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Terms and definitions
Other terms (continued)
Term
Definition
Impact severity level
(ISL)
A measure of the severity of an impact with a VRS using a
combination of vehicle acceleration and the theoretical head
impact velocity. 7, 8, 9
Inspection
The visual assessment of an object or substance.
Leaf pier
Supports that have a length along the carriageway similar to the
width of the main structure above, ignoring edge cantilevers.
Leaves (of an RBS)
The parts of an RBS which can be opened to enable traffic to
move from one side of the RBS to the other side of the RBS.
Legacy system
Permanent safety barriers, parapets and crash cushions
currently on the road network that were either manufactured and
installed before CE Marking under the Construction Products
Regulations ( 2011/305/EU [Ref 8.N]) became a statutory
requirement (for these products), or components of those
systems which have been replaced on a like for like basis.
Length of need
The minimum length of full containment VRS required to reduce
the risk to occupants of an errant vehicle and to other parties
that can be affected, to an 'acceptable' level. 10
Like-for-like
Replacing a specific component detailed in the manufacturer's
instruction or NPSBS v1 2005 [Ref 42.N] with the same specific
component, or with a variant permitted by the manufacturer, or
as described in NPSBS v1 2005 [Ref 42.N] or Section 4 of BS
7669-3 [Ref 91.N].
Longitudinal continuous
trafficable profile
A section of temporary safety barrier which is designed to be
driven upon without causing instability in the vehicle driving on it,
and which has been included in the vehicle path as part of the
full scale impact testing of the system.
Main structure
Any part of a bridge, viaduct, retaining wall or similar structure
upon which a vehicle, vehicle/pedestrian or pedestrian parapet is
mounted, including the plinth.
Manufacturer
Any person or entity that designs, manufactures, or markets a
road restraint system under their name or trademark.
Non re-directive crash
cushion
A crash cushion which has been successfully tested with test
approaches 1 to 3 inclusive, in accordance with BS EN 1317-1
[Ref 62.N] and BS EN 1317-3 [Ref 44.N].
Normalised values
(of dynamic deflection, working
width and vehicle intrusion)
Values that have been adjusted to take account of any
differences between the specified total mass of a vehicle, its
velocity and angle of approach, and the values measured during
full scale impact testing. 11
12
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Terms and definitions
Other terms (continued)
Term
Definition
Overload factor
A multiplier applied to design loads to account for uncertainties,
potential overloads, and safety margins, to understand whether
a structure or component could withstand unexpected or
extreme conditions
Parapet
A restraint system that is installed on the edge of a bridge,
retaining wall or similar elevated structure where there is a risk
due to the presence of a vertical drop.
Pedestrian parapet
A pedestrian restraint system that is installed on the edge of a
structure, such as a retaining wall, head wall or wing wall, where
there is a vertical or near vertical face 1.5 m or more in height
and where vehicular traffic is excluded, but where pedestrians,
cyclists and horse riders can be present.
Pedestrian restraint system
A system which is installed to provide a level of pedestrian
containment where pedestrian movement could occur due to
highway use or maintenance activities. 12
Pedestrian guardrail
A pedestrian restraint system that is installed along the edge of a
footway, footpath or on the edge of structures where there is a
vertical or near vertical face less than 1.5 m in height, intended to
restrain pedestrians, cyclists and horse riders from stepping onto
or crossing a road, or entering other areas likely to be hazardous.
Planned maintenance
Planned work required to parts of the motorway and all-purpose
trunk road network that have become unserviceable because of
general wear or tear, or due to a major upgrade, or changes to
parts of the network. 13, 14
Plinth
A continuous upstand upon which a vehicle, vehicle/pedestrian
or pedestrian parapet is mounted.
Pocketing
When an errant vehicle deflects a safety barrier, and then hits a
rigid object and is stopped abruptly in a bend in the deformed
barrier.
Point of no recovery (PNR)
The intersection point of a structure and a hazard which is:
1) adjacent to the structure; and
2) such that other parties can be affected (e.g the structure is
located over another road, or over a railway).
Point of set-back (Psb)
Point from which the set-back of an RRS is measured. 15
Railway authority
Authority responsible for the railway infrastructure.
13
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

PAGE 15 of 199

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Terms and definitions
Other terms (continued)
Term
Definition
Re-directive crash cushion
A crash cushion which has been successfully tested with test
approaches 1 to 4 inclusive for a directional crash cushion, and
tests 1 to 5 inclusive for a bidirectional crash cushion, in
accordance with BS EN 1317-1 [Ref 62.N] and BS EN 1317-3
[Ref 44.N].
Remote arrester bed
An arrester bed which is incorporated into a separate escape
lane leading off the main carriageway.
Removable barrier section (RBS)
A section of safety barrier, connected at both ends to sections of
standard safety barrier via transitions, which allows for removal
and reinstallation at pre-determined temporary openings, and
which, in the closed position, offers vehicle containment.
Rigid safety barrier
A safety barrier that when tested in accordance with BS EN
1317-1 [Ref 62.N] and BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N], does not
deflect from its pre-impact position, i.e. it has a dynamic
deflection of zero.
Road restraint system
(RRS)
General name for a vehicle restraint system or a pedestrian
restraint system used on the road. 16
Routine maintenance
Works which include all routine and cyclic work, and repairs
following damage to the RRS.
Running lane
That part of the trafficked carriageway nearest to the verge or
central reserve that is under consideration. 17
Safety barrier
A type of VRS installed alongside a road or on the central
reserve of a road which is typically comprised of metal and/or
concrete components.
Serviceable life
Life of an asset until its functional, physical, technological,
economical, social or legal condition or status (whichever is
sooner) dictates replacement.
Set-back
The distance between the Psb and the traffic face of a RRS. 18
Sidelong ground
Ground that falls away from the carriageway, where the road is
not on a formed embankment. 19
Smooth face
A face which has a surface finish with a maximum size of any
undulation or depression in the surface not exceeding 30mm,
when measured with respect to a plane through the peaks, the
plane being broadly parallel to the road alignment.
A structure having a 25mm wide chamfered construction joint in
its surface is also regarded as smooth.
Taper
Length over which there is a change in set-back in a VRS
14
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

PAGE 16 of 199

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Terms and definitions
Other terms (continued)
Term
Definition
Technical approval schedule
The schedule of documents to be used for the design or
assessment of a highway structure. 20
Temporary transition
A transition between two temporary VRS, or between a
temporary and a permanent VRS.
Temporary vehicle restraint
system
Vehicle restraint system that is to be in place for less than 4
years.
Terminal
Terminals are applied to the end of safety barriers to reduce the
risk of injury from the exposed ends of the safety barrier beams.
For some safety barriers, terminals also provide an anchorage to
the safety barrier.
A full height anchor based on legacy drawings is not considered
to be a terminal in the context of this document.
Transition
Interface between either two specified vehicle restraint systems
of different designs or performances, or between a specified
vehicle restraint system and a structure, that provides a gradual
change from the first to the second to reduce the risk of the
hazards from abrupt variations.
Vehicle arrester bed
Area of land adjacent to the road filled with a particular material
to decelerate and arrest errant vehicles.
Vehicle intrusion (VI)
The vehicle intrusion of an HGV is the maximum dynamic lateral
position from the undeformed traffic side of the barrier in
consideration of a notional load having the width and length of
the vehicle platform, and a total height of 4 m.
The vehicle intrusion of a bus is the maximum dynamic lateral
position of the bus from the undeformed traffic side of the barrier.
21
Vehicle intrusion class
The designation VI1, VI2, VI3, etc for classes of vehicle intrusion
levels, as defined in BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N].
Vehicle parapet
A vehicle restraint system that is installed on the edge of a
bridge, retaining wall or similar elevated structure where there is
a vertical drop.
Vehicle/pedestrian parapet
A vehicle restraint system that is installed on the edge of a
bridge, retaining wall or similar elevated structure where there is
a vertical drop, and which provides additional protection and
restraint for pedestrians and other road users.
Vehicle restraint system
(VRS)
A system which is installed on a road to provide a level of
containment for an errant vehicle, and which has been
successfully tested and/or assessed to the relevant part of EN13
17. 22
15
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Terms and definitions
Other terms (continued)
Term
Definition
Walking, cycling and horse-riding
(WCH) routes
All routes within the scope of CD 143 [Ref 17.N].
Working width (W)
The maximum lateral distance between any part of a safety
barrier on the undeformed traffic side, and the maximum
dynamic position of any part of the barrier during impact testing
to BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N]. 23, 24
Working width class
The designation W1, W2, W3, etc for classes of working width
levels, as defined in BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N].
Note 1: Additional details can be found in CD 127 [Ref 12.N].
Note 2: This includes designated lanes such as bus lanes and cycle lanes.
Note 3: The carriageway excludes hard shoulders and hard strips.
Note 4: This definition of carriageway can differ from those used in the UKPGA 1980/66 [Ref 38.I]
and UKSI 2016/362 [Ref 82.N].
Note 5: An expansion joint is a type of transition.
Note 6: 1 in 40 is the equivalent of 2.5%.
Note 7: Refer to BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N].
Note 8: ISL A affords a greater level of safety for the occupant of an errant vehicle than ISL B, and
ISL B affords a greater level of safety for the occupant of an errant vehicle than ISL C.
Note 9: ISL C exceeds ISL B, which in turn exceeds ISL A.
Note 10: When assessing whether the length of need and containment level are sufficient for a
temporary situation, the speed limit used in the RRRAP [Ref 81.N]is to be the temporary mandatory
limit in force, or the permanent speed limit if a temporary speed limit is not in place.
Note 11: Refer to BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N].
Note 12: Pedestrian restraint system includes both pedestrian parapets and pedestrian guardrails.
Note 13: This excludes work associated with incident damage and associated repairs.
Note 14: This includes work related to improvement schemes, covered by Section 278 of the UKPGA
1980/66 [Ref 38.I]and other third party works on or in close proximity to the network that affect risk to
road users and maintenance workers.
Note 15: Refer to CD 127 [Ref 12.N]for minimum requirements for the set-back of permanent vehicle
restraint systems.
Note 16: Figure A.1 shows the hierarchy of RRS.
Note 17: Under normal running conditions, any hard shoulder of a motorway would not be trafficked
and would therefore not be classed as the running lane. It can however become a temporary running
lane under temporary traffic management.
Note 18: Refer to CD 127 [Ref 12.N]for minimum requirements for the set-back of permanent vehicle
restraint systems.
Note 19: Sidelong ground typically occurs where the road is cut into the side of a hill such that the
road is effectively in cutting on one side and the ground drops away from the carriageway on the
other.
Note 20: Refer to CG 300 [Ref 73.N]for more information about the technical approval schedule.
Note 21: Further detail is given in BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N].
Note 22: A typical system consists of a terminal-safety barrier-terminal, or a terminal-safety
barrier-parapet-safety barrier-terminal, and includes transitions where appropriate.
Note 23: If the vehicle body deforms around the vehicle restraint system so that the latter cannot be
used for the purpose of measuring the working width, the maximum lateral position of any part of the
vehicle is the working width.
Note 24: Further detail is given in BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N].
16

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
1. Scope
1.
Scope
Aspects covered
1.1
This document details the requirements that shall be used for:
1)
permanent safety barriers;
2)
vehicle parapets;
3)
vehicle/pedestrian parapets;
4)
terminals;
5)
transitions;
6)
crash cushions;
7)
pedestrian parapets;
8)
pedestrian guardrails;
9)
removable barrier sections;
10)
temporary vehicle restraint systems;
11)
legacy road restraint systems;
12)
vehicle arrester beds;
13)
anti-glare systems mounted on road restraint systems; and
14)
cattle grids.
Implementation and application
1.2
This document shall be used according to the implementation requirements of GG 101 [Ref 40.N].
1.3
This document shall be applied on the Overseeing Organisation's motorways and all-purpose trunk
roads with a maximum speed limit of 50 mph or more, and two-way traffic flow of 5,000 annual average
daily traffic (AADT) or more:
1)
where the road is a new road;
2)
where the highway cross-section is being altered permanently;
3)
whenever the road restraint system (RRS) is life (serviceable life) expired and needs replacing;
4)
whenever the RRS needs replacing for any other reason;
5)
whenever a hazard is introduced and/or moved, and/or modified, changing the risk in the event of
a collision;
6)
whenever a RRS needs to be dismantled (other than where localised sections need to be
removed to gain access), e.g. during planned maintenance schemes; or
7)
where the Overseeing Organisation is responsible for a structure accommodating vehicles and/or
walking, cycling and horse-riding (WCH) users.
1.4
Unless instructed by the Overseeing Organisation and except for like-for-like renewal, this document
shall be applied:
1)
when other works (excluding routine maintenance) are being carried out near a hazard that is
currently without provision, or near an existing RRS that does not meet the requirements of this
document (e.g. with regard to its containment level, normalised working width class, normalised
vehicle intrusion class);
2)
when other works (excluding routine maintenance) are being carried out near an existing vehicle
restraint system (VRS) which is life (serviceable life) expired; and
3)
when other works (excluding routine maintenance) are being carried out near an existing RRS
that has less than 5 years serviceable life remaining and no other major maintenance works are
planned during the remaining life of the existing RRS.

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
1. Scope
1.4.1
Like-for-like renewal may be used where:
1)
localised sections need to be temporarily removed to gain access;
2)
repair work is required; or
3)
the RRS is required to be replaced due to incident damage.
NOTE
More details on localised sections which need to be temporarily removed to gain access can be found
in Section 10.
1.4.2
All RRS should be made compliant with current requirements where the works would not result in
undue additional expense and or delay.
1.5
Where VRS posts with height extension components are being removed and/or replaced, the posts
with height extension components shall not replaced on a like-for-like basis, and are to be replaced with
custom height posts manufactured to match the total height of the original extended posts.
1.6
Where terminals that incorporate welded angle beams are being removed and/or replaced, the welded
angle beams shall be replaced with a terminal in accordance with Section 6, or a terminal in
accordance with the NPSBS v1 2005 [Ref 42.N] drawings, and not on a like-for-like basis.
NOTE
In some cases, terminals might not be replaced at all if, for example, a gap is to be closed due to the
presence of unprotected hazards in advance of, or following, the terminal which is to be removed.
1.7
Where a length of safety barrier needs to be dismantled or replaced as part of planned maintenance
and the remaining length is less than 200 m, the entire length shall be installed in accordance with the
requirements of this document.
1.8
Where a safety barrier needs to be dismantled or replaced as part of planned maintenance between a
terminal and a transition into a parapet, or between two transitions into two parapets, and the remaining
length is less than 200 m, the entire length shall be installed in accordance with the requirements of this
document.

2. General requirements for road restraint syste...
2.
General requirements for road restraint systems
Hazard identification and risk mitigation for road restraint systems
2.1
All local hazards which are to be mitigated by the design, and their offset from the point of set-back
(Psb), shall be identified.
NOTE
A physical site inspection assists in identifying all local hazards which are to be mitigated by the design,
such as those hidden by vegetation, the presence of ditches, culverts and drainage features, and the
identification of hazards at the bottom of embankments, hence avoiding abortive work if all hazards are
not identified at an early stage.
2.2
On the all-purpose trunk road network where the speed limit is less than 50 mph and/or the two-way
traffic flow is less than 5,000 annual average daily traffic (AADT), a local risk assessment shall be
undertaken to determine:
1)
whether RRS is necessary; and
2)
the minimum performance levels for the RRS.
NOTE
Guidance on the specification of vehicle restraint systems on the approach to and across structures on
low speed and/or low flow roads can be found within Appendix B of this document.
2.3
For motorways and all-purpose trunk roads with both a speed limit of 50 mph or more and two-way
traffic flow of 5,000 AADT or more, the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] shall be used to formally record the type and
location of all of the hazards within the scheme extents which are to be mitigated by the design, both
existing hazards which are to be retained, and hazards which are to be added or modified as a result of
the scheme.
NOTE 1
The RRRAP [Ref 81.N] is a software tool which is available to assist in making an assessment, based
on risk, as to whether a VRS is warranted to reduce the risk of occupants of a vehicle from hitting near
side or offside hazards.
NOTE 2
In order to estimate the level of risk, the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] uses a combination of road data such as
the road type, speed limit and AADT, default data and factors for each hazard type, and user input
details relating to the nature and location of each hazard. The output shows the category of risk
(unacceptable, tolerable, or acceptable) without a safety barrier and with the optimum length of safety
barrier provision and its containment level. Indicative risk and benefit cost ratios can also be obtained.
2.4
A risk assessment shall be undertaken instead of the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] to determine whether RRS is
necessary, and the minimum performance levels for the RRS for:
1)
central reserves wider than 10 m;
2)
roads which run alongside railways;
3)
roundabouts and junction areas; and
4)
at lay-bys.
NOTE 1
The RRRAP [Ref 81.N] is not appropriate for an assessment of central reserves wider than 10 m, roads
which run alongside railways, roundabouts and junction areas or at lay-bys due to the complexity and
variability of hazards and their locations, traffic speed limits, road layouts and alignments, and the
variability of traffic incident data in these situations.
NOTE 2
The RRRAP [Ref 81.N] does not address the use of Emergency Crossing Points (ECPs), Maintenance
Crossing Points (MCPs), pedestrian restraint systems, vehicle arrester beds, anti-glare systems or
cattle grids.
2.5
Risk mitigation requirements contained in the DfT report 'Managing the accidental obstruction of the
railway by road vehicles' TAL 6/03 [Ref 83.N] shall be applied to roads which run alongside railways.
2.6
The RRRAP [Ref 81.N] shall be used to formally record the type and location of all of the hazards within
the scheme extents.
NOTE
Hazards within the scheme extents can be located outside of the highway boundary.

2. General requirements for road restraint syste...
2.6.1
Hazards that should be included in the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] include the following:
1)
above ground structural supports, bases or foundations positioned less than 3 m above the
adjacent paved carriageway;
2)
drainage culvert head walls;
3)
ditches where the depth of the ditch relative to the adjacent ground level is 1 m or greater;
4)
restricted headroom at a structure or part of a structure (refer to CD 127 [Ref 12.N]);
5)
surface of a rigid structure or construction, including retaining and abutment walls, that do not
have a smooth face adjacent to the traffic that extends 1.5 m or more above the adjacent
carriageway level;
6)
exposed rock-faced cutting slopes, rock-filled gabions, crib walling or similar structures;
7)
cutting slopes and earth bunds greater than 1 m high and with a side slope gradient of 1 in 1 or
steeper;
8)
embankments and vertical drops;
9)
kerbs greater than 75 mm in height;
10)
steps with or without handrails;
11)
vehicle and vehicle/pedestrian parapets;
12)
environmental barriers or screens;
13)
highway fences and walls, including boundary fences;
14)
dwarf retaining walls;
15)
permanent or expected water hazard with a depth of water of 0.6 m or more, including rivers,
reservoirs, stilling ponds or lakes;
16)
road lighting columns, not certified as meeting the requirements of BS EN 12767 [Ref 43.N];
17)
high-mast road lighting columns;
18)
sign and signal gantry supports including variable message signs;
19)
sign/signal posts not certified as meeting the requirements of BS EN 12767 [Ref 43.N] or which
are not deemed to comply with BS EN 12767 [Ref 43.N];
20)
sign plates greater than 2 m in height, located in a position where the bottom of the sign plate is
less than 1.5 m above the adjacent carriageway;
21)
above-ground communications control cabinets, pillars and equipment (excluding emergency
telephones), CCTV masts and telephone masts (refer to CD 354 [Ref 15.N], and TD 131 [Ref
66.N]);
22)
stores for emergency/diversion signs and similar structures;
23)
wooden telegraph poles;
24)
a tree or trees having, or expected to have at maturity, trunk girths (circumference) of 250 mm or
more, measured at a height of 0.3 m above ground level (refer to LD 117 [Ref 45.I]);
25)
existing road restraint systems which are to remain in place;
26)
pedestrian restraint systems not certified as meeting the requirements of BS EN 12767 [Ref
43.N];
27)
hazards where other parties could be at risk of injury from an errant vehicle, such as:
a)
subway entrance for WCH users or agricultural underbridge passing under the highway due
to the risk of a fall from height;
b)
a railway, canal or separate road or carriageway;
c)
public meeting places where a number of people are present for a significant time, including
bus shelters, places of worship, schools, hospitals, recreational, retail facilities or factories;
d)
chemical works, petroleum storage tanks or depots, domestic gas canisters or tanks, solar
farms, battery storage facilities, facilities manufacturing or storing hazardous materials in
bulk; or
21
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

2. General requirements for road restraint syste...
e)
other infrastructure, where the impact on the community / society as a whole is
disproportional to the damage caused, including significant utility (electrical, gas) or
communications infrastructure.
NOTE
Although vehicle and vehicle/pedestrian parapets can form part of the VRS, their traffic-facing ends are
a hazard, and their presence needs to be identified.
2.7
The decision to exclude any hazards from the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] assessment shall be recorded in the
design record, taking into account the probability of an errant vehicle reaching the hazard.
2.8
Risk mitigation measures shall be applied to each of the identified hazards, in accordance with the
hierarchy outlined in Table 2.8.
Table 2.8 Risk mitigation measures
Priority
Risk mitigation measure
1
Eliminate the risk.
2
Reduce the risk of impacting the hazard by relocating the hazard to a position posing
less overall risk and/or by redesigning the hazard to make it less aggressive.
3
Isolate the risk posed by the hazard.
4
Control the risk.
NOTE 1
Examples of 'priority 1' are to remove an existing hazard, or to not install a new hazard.
NOTE 2
Examples of 'priority 2' are to relocate the hazard to a position posing less overall risk, and/or by
redesigning the hazard to make it less aggressive and/or to install a passively safe structure.
NOTE 3
Examples of 'priority 3' are to conduct a further review of hazard location, potential impact angles, and
moving hazards to limit overall exposure extents (e.g. by clustering hazards together).
NOTE 4
Examples of 'priority 4' are to install an RRS, permanent and or temporary VRS, and improve or
increase signage, markings and or lighting.
2.9
The hierarchy of risk mitigation options shall be applied in sequence, starting at 'priority 1' as shown in
Table 2.8.
2.9.1
Where it is not possible to provide a risk mitigation option given in Table 2.8, a lower priority risk
mitigation measure may be used.
2.10
Where a lower priority risk mitigation measure is used, the reason(s) why the higher priority risk
mitigation measure could not be used shall be recorded in the design strategy record (or equivalent).
2.11
Where a hazard is to be mitigated by risk mitigation measure priority 1, 2 or 3 as identified in Table 2.8,
or by another risk mitigation measure, the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] inputs shall be updated to reflect these
changes.
NOTE
Following consideration of risk mitigation measures, the updated RRRAP [Ref 81.N] output can indicate
that the VRS is no longer required or that the performance characteristics or length of the VRS can be
modified.
2.12
Where a hazard is not to be mitigated by risk mitigation measure priority 1, 2 or 3 as identified in Table
2.8, for each defined hazard, the need for a VRS, its minimum lengths and minimum performance
requirements shall be given by the output from the RRRAP [Ref 81.N].
2.13
The initial design shall be based on an output from the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] that produces a level of risk
which is so far as is reasonably practicable (SFAIRP).
NOTE
The output from the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] will not necessarily be the final design, and additional factors,
outlined in subsequent clauses, will need to be assessed in the development of the final design.
2.14
The design of the RRS shall:
22
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

1)
accommodate its safe and efficient installation, maintenance, repair, and removal;
2)
allow access to and maintenance of above and below ground services, equipment and other
highway assets, including temporary signs; and
3)
allow for the safe and efficient maintenance of the adjacent verge/central reserve.
2.15
The design of the RRS layout shall enable all road restraints to be installed in accordance with the
manufacturer's product description and associated installation instructions.
2.15.1
To enable all RRS to be installed in accordance with the manufacturer's product description and
associated installation instructions it may be necessary for the designer to discuss their initial design
with the installer, supplier or manufacturer of the RRS to identify whether product(s) are available which
could be installed so as to meet the initial design.
NOTE
The initial design can require modification where the RRS cannot be installed in accordance with the
manufacturer's product description and associated installation instructions and meet the initial design.
2.16
The design of the RRS layout shall take account of and minimise the interaction/interference with other
roadside services such as drainage, kerbing, reinforcement in structures, communications and power
cables, and other existing services.
2.16.1
Inspection covers should not be installed directly underneath the RRS.
2.17
The design of the RRS layout shall take account of, and minimise secondary risk factors to a level
which is SFAIRP.
NOTE
Examples of secondary risk factors include the trajectory of a vehicle which is contained and redirected
by a vehicle restraint system and/or the trajectory of detached parts (such as a detached anti-glare
screen).
2.18
A record of the design for hazard mitigation shall include the assumptions and design decisions made
to mitigate each of these hazards to demonstrate that the design meets the requirements of this
document.
2.19
During the preliminary and detailed design stages, the cost of providing VRS shall be quantified against:
1)
the cost of replacing the asset with a passively safe alternative (where a passively safe
alternative is available);
2)
moving the asset such that VRS is not required; and
3)
the costs and implications of the asset being out of service or unavailable for a period of time.
Documentation requirements for each adopted hazard mitigation layout
2.20
The following documentation shall be provided for each adopted hazard mitigation layout:
1)
a record of the design, including the assumptions made to mitigate each of the hazards to
demonstrate that the design meets the requirements of this document, and where a lower priority
risk mitigation measure is used, the reason(s) why the higher priority risk mitigation measure is
not being used;
2)
the risk assessment and/or the 'Full Report' output from the RRRAP; and
3)
a completed contract specific specification.
2.21
All RRS included within the design shall be allocated a unique ID within the design drawings.
NOTE
This will enable each of the RRS within the design to be identified within the works specific
requirements.
2.22
The unique ID of each RRS shall be given as a design output.
NOTE
The unique ID is solely for the purpose of the design drawings, and not for the handover of inventory to
client.

2. General requirements for road restraint syste...
Road safety audits
2.23
All stage road safety audits shall be conducted in accordance with GG 119 [Ref 63.N].
General requirements for road restraint systems
2.24
Where an installation of a continuous nature comprises of different RRS, all of the RRS shall be
compatible in their functionality.
NOTE
For example, a safety barrier could require the connecting terminals or transitions to provide an
anchorage.
2.25
RRS shall be able to achieve the performance included as design outputs at the range of temperatures
between Tmax and Tmin as defined in BS EN 1991-1-5 1997 [Ref 22.N] and the NA to BS EN 1991-1-5
[Ref 90.N].
2.26
Unless specifically indicated otherwise by the manufacturer's product description and associated
installation instructions, direct contact between dissimilar metals shall not be permitted.
2.27
Unless specifically indicated otherwise by the manufacturer's product description and associated
installation instructions, direct contact between uncoated aluminium and timber treated with a
preservative containing copper shall be avoided by interposing a separating layer.
2.28
Unless specifically indicated otherwise by the manufacturer's product description and associated
installation instructions, the design of the RRS shall be such that no drilling, cutting (including flame
cutting) or welding of beams and posts is required after the corrosion protection has been applied.
2.29
The requirements of CG 300 [Ref 73.N] shall be met where an RRS is to be installed on a structure.
2.30
Unless specifically indicated otherwise by the manufacturer's product description and associated
installation instructions, where high strength bedding mortar is required under metal baseplates or
under precast concrete parapet elements, the design outputs for the mortar material shall be specified
in accordance with Appendix G of CD 350 [Ref 79.N].
2.31
Unless specifically indicated otherwise by the manufacturer's product description and associated
installation instructions, where ancillary concrete is required for repairs to or replacement of permanent
legacy VRS systems, the design outputs for the ancillary concrete material shall be specified in
accordance with Appendix G of CD 350 [Ref 79.N].
Locating roadside furniture in front of, or in advance of, new and existing VRS
2.32
Road furniture which is not passively safe shall not be positioned in front (i.e. within the set-back) of a
new or existing VRS.
2.32.1
Passively safe road furniture may be positioned on the carriageway side of a new or existing VRS if the
distance from the traffic face of the VRS to the traffic face of the passively safe road furniture or
equipment (measured perpendicular to the carriageway) is equal to or greater than one third of the total
height of the passively safe road furniture or equipment.
NOTE 1
This can reduce the risk of the furniture or equipment touching and damaging the VRS if hit, or acting
as a ramp over the VRS.
NOTE 2
As an example a 3 m tall passively safe sign support can be located 1 m in front of the traffic face of a
safety barrier (measured perpendicular to the carriageway).
2.33
Road furniture shall not be positioned less than 6 m in advance of a new or existing crash cushion or a
terminal.
NOTE
This includes road furniture which is passively safe.
Drainage, kerbs and the positioning of RRS
2.34
Positioning of RRS components relative to infrastructure including drainage and kerbs shall be in
accordance with the RRS manufacturer's product description and associated installation instructions.
24
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

2. General requirements for road restraint syste...
2.35
Positioning of RRS components relative to infrastructure including drainage and kerbs shall be such
that it does not compromise the durability or effectiveness of the RRS.
2.36
Positioning of RRS components relative to infrastructure including drainage and kerbs shall be such
that it does not compromise the durability, hydraulic performance and maintenance of the drainage
system, or effectiveness of the drainage infrastructure.
NOTE
The presence of RRS within drainage infrastructure can compromise the durability, hydraulic
performance and maintenance of the drainage system, or effectiveness of the drainage infrastructure.
Further requirements regarding the positioning of RRS components relative to drainage infrastructure
can be found within CD 521 [Ref 44.I] and CD 524 [Ref 18.N].
Set-back for permanent VRS
2.37
CD 127 [Ref 12.N] shall be used for set-back requirements for permanent VRS.
2.38
The rate of change of set-back shall not be greater than that declared in the manufacturer's product
description and associated installation instructions.
2.39
For non-proprietary systems, the rate of change of set-back shall not be greater than 1 in 16 for
deformable safety barriers, or 1 in 20 for rigid safety barriers.
General design requirements for RRS anchorages, foundations and structural supports
2.40
Anchorages shall not be of an expanding type, other than undercut anchorages.
2.41
Unless otherwise indicated in the manufacturer's product description and associated installation
instructions, anchorages shall include an internally threaded component to receive the attachment
system.
2.42
All parts of anchorages on bridge decks and other structures (where the anchorage is within 80 mm of
the upper surface of the supporting concrete or where the anchorage parts are threaded to receive the
holding down bolt) shall be of stainless steel designation 1.4401, 1.4436, 1.4362 or 1.4462, or duplex
stainless steel designation 1.4362, to BS EN 10088-5 [Ref 69.N].
2.43
Holding down bolts, screws, studs, and nuts on bridge decks and other structures shall be stainless
steel grade A4-80 or grade D4-80 to BS EN ISO 3506-1 [Ref 26.N]and BS EN ISO 3506-2 [Ref 27.N].
2.44
The dimensions and tolerances of the bolts, screws and nuts shall conform to BS EN ISO 4016 [Ref
32.N], BS EN ISO 4018 [Ref 33.N] and BS EN ISO 4034 [Ref 34.N].
2.45
Washers shall conform to BS EN ISO 898-3 2021 [Ref 28.N], BS EN ISO 7091 2000 [Ref 51.N], BS EN
ISO 7092 2000 [Ref 52.N], BS EN ISO 7093-1 2000 [Ref 49.N], BS EN ISO 7093-2 2000 [Ref 50.N]
and BS EN ISO 7094 2000 [Ref 48.N].
2.46
Isolation washers excluded, washers on bridge decks and other structures shall be made from
stainless steel strip designation 1.4401 or 1.4436 to BS EN 10029 [Ref 39.N], BS EN 10048 [Ref 38.N],
BS EN 10051 [Ref 11.N], BS EN ISO 9445-1 [Ref 9.N]and BS EN ISO 9445-2 [Ref 10.N].
2.47
Any particular requirements or restrictions of the RRS with respect to the structure or foundations, or
conversely, requirements or restrictions of the structure or foundations with respect to the RRS shall be
given as a design output.
2.47.1
The design output may include the nominal loads (direct forces, maximum dead and impact loads,
moments and co-existent shears and their points of origin) transmitted from the RRS to the structure or
foundations.
2.48
The limits of nominal loads that are to be resisted by the structure, foundations or existing anchorage
system from the RRS, and the points of application of those loads shall be included as design outputs
for the nominal load for on-site anchorage load tests.
2.49
Anchorages or foundation systems shall enable the RRS to perform to the declared or stated level of
performance, regardless of temperature or other environmental variations, throughout the serviceable
life of the proposed RRS.

Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
2. General requirements for road restraint syste...
2.50
Any geometric restrictions of either the RRS or the structure or foundations shall be given as a design
output for loading and anchorage requirements.
2.51
The anchorage method for a RRS on a structure shall be chosen so as to minimise the risk of damage
to reinforcement within the structure or waterproofing on the structure during the installation process.
2.51.1
To minimise the risk of damage to reinforcement within the structure or waterproofing on the structure
during the installation process, a reinforcement survey may be required prior to the installation of the
RRS.
Additional corrosion protection system for RRS
2.52
The need for any additional corrosion protection system which needs to be applied to the RRS shall be
included as a design output.
2.52.1
Additional corrosion protection systems may be required where the RRS is installed in an environment
which is more corrosive, such as when the RRS is connected directly to a structure, inside a tunnel, or
under a bridge, particularly in locations on the RRS where water is likely to collect and not be removed
by evaporation.
2.53
Additional corrosion protection systems shall not be required for plastic permanent safety barriers,
plastic components of multi-material safety barriers or wooden components of combined metal/wood
permanent safety barriers.
Additional coatings and treatments for RRS
2.54
Any additional coatings or treatments which need to be applied to the RRS for any reason other than
corrosion protection shall be included as a design output.
NOTE
Reasons for additional coatings or treatments to RRS other than for corrosion protection include
improving visibility or the aesthetics of the RRS.
Protection of walkers, cyclists and horse riders
2.55
Where an RRS is to be located adjacent to a walking, cycling or horse-riding (WCH) route, the need to
provide protection to the walkers, cyclists or horse riders shall be included as a design output.
2.55.1
RRS adjacent to WCH routes should not have any features which could cause injury to a passing
walker, cyclist or horse-rider.
Environmental requirements for RRS
2.56
Environmental factors relevant to the installation, maintenance and demolition of the RRS that will
affect the choice, positioning, and type or material for the RRS, shall be included as a design output.
NOTE 1
Environmental factors can include snow or sand (where, for example, a safety barrier with a solid traffic
face can give rise to levels of drifting and difficulties in snow or sand clearance), or marine
environments (where metal products can be subject to high levels of corrosion and concrete can be
adversely affected).
NOTE 2
Environmental factors can also include a local prevalence of wildlife, so a suitable choice of barrier can
ensure that animals are able to leave the carriageway and are not trapped by the RRS. In such
circumstances, it can be more appropriate to install fencing in the verge to reduce the risk of animals
entering the carriageway in the first place.
NOTE 3
Environmental factors can also include vandalism, unauthorised access, anti-social behaviour
(throwing objects from structures) and/or social gatherings (including car parking or 'on-foot'
gatherings) any of which can have a direct effect on the design outputs.
Additional requirements for RRS
2.57
Any factors relevant to the installation, maintenance, repair and demolition/removal of the RRS that will
influence the choice of product shall be included as a design output.

2. General requirements for road restraint syste...
2.58
Where an assessment has identified that the RRS is no longer needed or its required containment level
and/or length is reduced, the removal of the RRS and/or the revised performance characteristics for the
RRS, shall be included as a design output.
Minimum serviceable life (durability) requirements for RRS
2.59
All components of RRS shall have a minimum serviceable life of the values given in Table 2.59.
Table 2.59 Minimum serviceable life for RRS
RRS
Minimum serviceable life (in
years)
Permanent metal safety barriers, terminals, transitions, crash
cushions and removable barrier sections
20
Permanent concrete safety barriers
50
Plastic permanent safety barriers
10
Plastic components for multi-material safety barriers
10
Wooden components for combined metal and wooden
permanent safety barriers
10
Metal vehicle parapets and metal components of combined
metal and concrete vehicle parapets
30
Concrete vehicle parapets and concrete components of
combined metal and concrete vehicle parapets
120
Metal pedestrian parapets, and metal components of

The use of passively safe road furniture as an alternative to VRS
2.60
Passively safe road furniture shall be used in accordance with the National Annex to BS EN 12767 [Ref
43.N]as an alternative to protecting the hazard with VRS:
1)
unless there is another hazard at the location which is not being removed, relocated or made
passively safe and that requires the provision of VRS;
2)
at roundabouts or junctions where there is insufficient room for full VRS provision; or
3)
where VRS will be vulnerable to full frontal impact or where the VRS will not be installed with the
correct orientation for all the anticipated directions of traffic movement.
NOTE 1
The design output requirements for passively safe road furniture are given in CD 354 [Ref 15.N].

3. Requirements for permanent safety barriers
3.
Requirements for permanent safety barriers
General requirements for permanent safety barriers
3.1
Safety barriers installed alongside or within a motorway and/or all-purpose trunk road shall reduce the
consequences of vehicles leaving the carriageway and entering areas where hazards exist.
3.2
Safety barriers shall contain and redirect vehicles along the line of the barrier in the direction of travel,
so the vehicles do not rotate or overturn.
3.3
The safety barrier layout shall enable all safety barriers to be installed in accordance with the
manufacturer's product description and associated installation instructions.
3.3.1
Advice on the safety barrier design may be sought from safety barrier manufacturers to identify whether
products are available which meet the preliminary design.
3.4
Unless required by the manufacturer's product description and associated installation instructions,
sockets shall be left uncapped and passive filler placed in all sockets.
NOTE
Caps are not to be located around posts installed in sockets as this can trap moisture within the
sockets, increasing the rate of degradation of the posts.
3.5
The range of heights over which the safety barrier needs to be effective and the level(s) relative to
which they are to be set, shall be in accordance with the manufacturers' specification and be included
as design outputs.
Minimum containment levels for permanent safety barriers
3.6
On roads with a speed limit of 50 mph or more, the minimum containment level for permanent safety
barriers shall be N2 unless a site specific risk assessment identifies that a minimum containment level
greater than N2 is required.
3.7
On roads with a speed limit of less than 50 mph, the minimum containment level for permanent safety
barriers shall be N1 unless a site specific risk assessment identifies that a minimum containment level
greater than N1 is required.
3.8
A site specific risk assessment shall include the output from the RRRAP [Ref 81.N].
NOTE
A site specific risk assessment can include, for example, the output from a CS 453 [Ref 75.N]or a CS
461 [Ref 2.N]risk assessment.
3.9
Where a site specific risk assessment identifies that a containment level greater than the minimum level
is required, the greater containment level shall be included as a design output.
3.10
Where a site specific risk assessment identifies that a very high containment level is required, an H4a
containment safety barrier shall be included as a design output.
3.11
Where the need for a higher containment level or very high containment level safety barrier has been
identified, the nature of the risk, any mitigation with the steps taken to reduce the risk and the resulting
containment level required shall be recorded in the design strategy record (or equivalent).
Impact severity level (ISL) for permanent safety barriers
3.12
The impact severity level (ISL) for permanent safety barriers shall be either ISL A or ISL B.
3.12.1
ISL A should be selected over ISL B as it affords a lower level of injury risk to the occupants of an
errant vehicle than ISL B.
Normalised working width classes and normalised vehicle intrusion classes for
permanent safety barriers
3.13
With the exception of passively safe road furniture, hazards shall not be located within the normalised
working width class and, for higher and very high containment safety barriers, the normalised vehicle
intrusion class, of a safety barrier.
29
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 31 of 199
======================================================================
CD 377 Version 5.0.0
3. Requirements for permanent safety barriers
NOTE
An emergency telephone is not considered to be a hazard, and they can be installed within the
normalised working width class and, for higher and very high containment safety barriers, the
normalised vehicle intrusion class, of a safety barrier.
3.14
For normal containment level safety barriers, the maximum identified value of normalised working width
class that the local hazard(s) allow, shall be included as a design output.
3.15
For normal containment level safety barriers, where the maximum identified value of normalised
working width that the local hazard(s) allow is less than 0.6m, the maximum identified value of
normalised working width shall be included as a design output.
3.16
An RRS shall not be placed within the normalised working width class or normalised vehicle intrusion
class of an adjacent safety barrier other than where a double sided safety barrier is designed to
bifurcate into two separate single sided safety barriers.
3.17
For higher and very high containment level safety barriers, the maximum identified values of
normalised working width class and normalised vehicle intrusion class that the local hazard(s) allow,
shall be included as a design output.
3.18
For higher and very high containment level safety barriers, where the maximum identified value of
normalised working width or normalised vehicle intrusion that the local hazard(s) allow is less than
0.6m, the maximum identified value of normalised working width or normalised vehicle intrusion shall
be included as a design output.
3.19
For all higher and very high containment level safety barriers, the required clearance to any hazard that
is vulnerable to residual loading shall be included as a design output.
3.20
For double sided safety barrier installed between two carriageways, the normalised working width class
shall not exceed the distance from the traffic face of the barrier, to the traffic face of the white line on the
opposite carriageway or, if there is no white line, to the traffic face of the kerb or edge of carriageway.
Passively safe road furniture in the normalised working width class of a safety barrier
3.21
Passively safe road furniture placed within the normalised working width class of a safety barrier shall
have an energy absorption category of non energy absorbing (NE), as defined by BS EN 12767 [Ref
43.N].
3.22
Where passively safe road furniture is to be located within the normalised working width class of a
single sided safety barrier in the verge, it shall be demonstrated that:
1)
it is not possible to locate the passively safe furniture outside of the normalised working width
class of the existing safety barrier;
2)
it is not possible to install a safety barrier with a smaller normalised working width class;
3)
a risk assessment has identified that a safety barrier is required due to a hazard(s) other than the
passively safe furniture, and it is not possible to remove the safety barrier; and
4)
the arrangement does not present a risk of a secondary incident.
3.23
Where passively safe sign/signal posts (excluding those with slip bases) are to be located within the
normalised working width class of a single sided safety barrier in the central reserve, it shall be
demonstrated that:
1)
it is not possible to locate the passively sign/signal posts outside of the normalised working width
class of the existing safety barrier;
2)
it is not possible to install a safety barrier with a smaller normalised working width class;
3)
a risk assessment has identified that a safety barrier is required due to a hazard(s) other than the
passively safe sign/signal posts, and it is not possible to remove the safety barrier; and
4)
the arrangement does not present a risk of a secondary incident.

32

3. Requirements for permanent safety barriers
3.25
The minimum length of need shall be the minimum length of full containment safety barrier in advance
of the hazard(s) (see length B to C in Figure 3.24), plus the length of the hazard(s) (see length C to D in
Figure 3.24), plus the minimum length of full containment safety barrier beyond the hazard(s) (see
length D to E in Figure 3.24).
3.25.1
Where the road passes over a structure, part of the length of need may be provided by a vehicle
parapet, connected to lengths of safety barrier via transitions as long as all of the VRS systems have,
or exceed, the minimum containment level.
3.25.2
Part of the length of need may be provided by a transition(s) provided that the transition has, or
exceeds, the minimum containment level.
3.26
The minimum length of full containment safety barrier in advance of the hazard(s) that produces an
"acceptable" level of risk through the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] shall be used as the basis for the final design
(see length B to C in Figure 3.24).
3.27
The minimum length of full containment safety barrier beyond the hazard(s) that produces an
"acceptable" level of risk through the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] shall be used as the basis for the final design
(see length D to E in Figure 3.24).
NOTE 1
The length D to E returned by the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] can typically be 0m for one way roads and roads
with a vehicle restraint system in the central reserve.
NOTE 2
For single carriageways with two-way flow, the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] outputs the minimum length of full
containment safety barrier in advance of the hazard(s) that produces an "acceptable" level of risk (see
length B to C in Figure 3.24), as well as the minimum length of full containment safety barrier beyond
the hazard(s) (see length D to E in Figure 3.24).
3.28
The minimum length of full containment safety barrier in advance of, or beyond, the hazard(s) shall be
increased if there are local factors which mean that the minimum lengths from the RRRAP [Ref 81.N]
do not produce an "acceptable" level of risk once those local factors have been incorporated into the
design.
NOTE
Local factors could include the presence of bends, junctions, roundabouts, super elevations, visibility
and stopping sight distance issues.
3.29
The minimum total length of safety barrier (see length A to F in Figure 3.24) shall be the length of need
plus the additional lengths declared by the safety barrier manufacturer in advance of, and after the
length of need, which are required to ensure that the safety barrier attains full containment at points B
and E in Figure 3.24.
NOTE
The additional lengths declared by the safety barrier manufacturer in advance of, and after the length of
need, which are required to ensure that the safety barrier attains full containment at points B and E in
Figure 3.24 are product specific.
3.30
The minimum total length of safety barrier (as shown by length A to F in Figure 3.24) shall be included
as a design output.
NOTE
Examples of determining the minimum total length of safety barrier are given in Appendix C.
3.31
Where a safety barrier manufacturer declares a minimum length of full containment safety barrier in
advance of, or beyond, a hazard(s), or a minimum length of need, or a minimum total length, the longer
of the manufacturer's values and those calculated above shall be incorporated into the final design.
Layout and alignment of permanent safety barriers
3.32
The proposed design layout of the safety barrier shall be such that :
1)
it has a flowing alignment along the length of the safety barrier, with any changes in alignment
being gradual;
2)
apart from at roundabouts and at junctions, changes in the angle of the safety barrier presented
to oncoming traffic (i.e. the approach angle) are not going to be significantly different in effect on
an errant vehicle or on the safety barrier, to the angle(s) of approach at which the safety barrier
has been tested or those declared by the manufacturer;
33
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 35 of 199
======================================================================
CD 377 Version 5.0.0
3. Requirements for permanent safety barriers
3)
changes in alignment do not give rise to a pocketing effect.
3.33
On the approach to structures, if the taper or part of the taper is included in the length of need, the
structure shall have already been assessed as Group 3 in accordance with CS 453 [Ref 75.N], or
designed to withstand main load conditions in accordance with BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N].
3.33.1
At locations other than the approach to structures which have already been assessed as Group 3 in
accordance with CS 453 [Ref 75.N], or designed to withstand main load conditions in accordance with
BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N], any taper catering for changes in set-back should not be placed beyond
point B on the approach and in advance of point D on the departure to a hazard, as shown in Figure
3.24.
3.33.2
Where the hazard is long in length (e.g. at the top of an embankment requiring safety barrier), the
set-back may change within the length of the hazard.
Gaps between lengths of permanent safety barrier
3.34
Safety barrier layouts shall be planned to minimise the number of leading ends of safety barriers.
3.35
Gaps of 50 m or less between adjacent safety barrier installations shall be closed and the safety barrier
made continuous, unless the gaps are required for access or maintenance, or the safety barriers are at
different offsets where there is a change in set-back which is not accommodated by the manufacturers
declared rates of taper.
3.36
Gaps greater than 50m and up to 100m in length between adjacent safety barrier installations shall be
closed and the safety barrier made continuous, unless there are significant cost or technical
requirements for the gap to remain open, or unless the gaps are required for access or maintenance, or
the safety barriers are at different offsets where there is a change in set-back which is not
accommodated by the manufacturers declared rates of taper.
3.37
Where it is not possible to physically close a gap between safety barrier installations due to the safety
barriers being at different offsets, where there is a change in set-back which is not accommodated by
the manufacturers declared rates of taper, or where maintenance or access for WCH users is required,
the installations shall be arranged to minimise the risk of a vehicle impacting the first safety barrier and
being directed into the leading terminal of the second safety barrier, or into the hazard that the second
safety barrier is protecting.
The positioning of permanent safety barriers at slopes
3.38
The verge and central reserve below and immediately in front of and behind the safety barrier shall be
without abrupt changes in level unless permitted by the safety barrier manufacturer's product
description and associated installation instructions.
3.39
The crest of a slope which exceeds 200mm in height shall not be within the normalised working width
class of the safety barrier, as shown in Figure 3.39, unless permitted by the safety barrier
manufacturer's product description and associated installation instructions..

On embankments and sidelong ground where the proximity of the safety barrier to the crest of the slope

distance of the post to the top of slope in the safety barrier manufacturer's product description and
associated installation instructions shall be included as a design output under ground conditions.

Motorcyclist protection devices
3.42
Where an increased level of injury risk due to loss of control to motorcyclists is identified, appropriate
mitigation measures compliant with PD CEN/TS 17342 [Ref 59.N] shall be included as a design output,
subject to a risk assessment.
NOTE
There could be an increased level of injury risk due to loss of control to motorcyclists from barriers on
slip roads, loops and link roads, especially where there are curves with a radius of less than 100m, or a
left-hand bend.
3.43
Where it is proposed to attach a motorcyclist protection device to a permanent safety barrier, it shall be
35

checked that the attachment of the motorcyclist protection device will not adversely affect the dynamic
impact performance of the permanent safety barrier.
The risk of falling materials/rocks
3.44
Where there is a risk of falling materials/rocks reaching the verge, mitigation shall be put in place to
ensure the safety barrier is not required to contain or restrain this material unless the safety barrier had
zero deflection during impact testing to BS EN 1317-1 [Ref 62.N] and BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N].
3.45
Any mitigation to reduce the risk of falling materials/rocks shall not affect the performance
characteristics of the safety barrier.
Safety barrier provision at structural supports
3.46
Structural supports, such as bridge piers shall be assessed in accordance with CS 453 [Ref 75.N]
(existing structures) or designed in accordance with BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] (new structures).
NOTE
Abutments are not normally considered to be at risk as a result of vehicle collision as they are assumed
to have sufficient mass to withstand the collision loads for global purposes; (refer to CS 453 [Ref 75.N]
for existing structural supports, or BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] for new structures).
3.47
The minimum containment level of a safety barrier in front of an structural support shall be N2.
NOTE
Where a normal containment level safety barrier is required, the safety barrier is generally not intended
to provide protection for the structure, only to reduce the risk of injury to road users.
3.48
Where a CS 453 [Ref 75.N] assessment has been used to assess an existing structural support and a
safety barrier is to be provided in front of the structural support, the minimum containment level of the
safety barrier shall be as in Table 3.48, and given as a design output.
Table 3.48 Minimum containment level of safety barrier in front of existing structural supports
Categorisation of existing structural support according to CS
453 [Ref 75.N]
Minimum containment level
Group 1a or 1b (particularly at risk)
H4a
Group 2 (at risk)
H1
3.49
Where BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] has been used to design a new structural support, the minimum
containment of the safety barrier in front of the structural support.shall be in accordance with PD
6688-1-7 2014 [Ref 55.N], and given as a design output.
3.50
Where a safety barrier is required in front of a structural support, and a minimum containment level of
N2 is required, the normalised working width class shall be given as a design output.
3.51
Where a safety barrier is required in front of a structural support, and a minimum containment level of
H1 or H4a is required, the normalised working width class and normalised vehicle intrusion class shall
be given as design outputs.
3.52
Where a safety barrier is installed under a structure, the extent of a safety barrier's normalised working
width class shall be no closer to the structure than the point where the maintained headroom is lost, as
shown in Figure 3.52.

3.53
Where the minimum headroom to an overbridge in the verge is less than the required maintained

3.55
The minimum length of need in advance of, and beyond, a structural support in the central reserve shall

3.55.1
The use of the RRRAP to determine the minimum length of need in advance of, and/or beyond, a

3. Requirements for permanent safety barriers
NOTE
Figure 3.56 shows the central reserve situation. For verge situations, the arrangement is similar, but
with only one double sided safety barrier on the approach and departure to the structural support.
3.56.1
Where it is not possible to incorporate the arrangements in Figures 3.54 and Figure 3.56 due to an
insufficient cross sectional width to install a safety barrier in front of the structural support, a safety
barrier may be installed in line with the face of a structural support, providing the following conditions
apply:
1)
the abutment or structure has been assessed/designed for impact load in accordance with CS
453 [Ref 75.N] (existing structures) or BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] (new designs);
2)
the structural support has a smooth face, with a minimum height of 1.5m;
3)
in the case of bridge piers, the pier is a leaf pier;
4)
the structural support's geometric design is such that it does not lean towards on-coming traffic
(i.e. in the direction of traffic); and
5)
approved transitions are used between the safety barrier and the structural support.
3.56.2
Where it is not possible to incorporate the arrangements in Figure 3.54 and Figure 3.56 due to an
insufficient cross sectional width to install a safety barrier in front of the structural support, a safety
barrier may be installed in line with a collar constructed around the base of a structural support,
providing the following conditions apply:
1)
the concrete collar has been assessed/designed for impact load in accordance with CS 453 [Ref
75.N] (existing collars) or BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] (new designs); and
2)
the concrete collar has a smooth face, with a minimum height of 1.5m.
Safety barrier provision at gantries
3.57
The safety barrier containment levels at gantries shall be determined by the outcome of the risk
assessment from CD 365 [Ref 53.N] and the UK National Annex NA to BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 88.N], as
agreed with the Overseeing Organisation.
Safety barrier provision at vehicle parapets
3.58
A safety barrier shall be provided to prevent direct impact with each end of the vehicle parapet.
3.59
Where a safety barrier is to connect into a vehicle parapet, a transition compliant with the requirements
of Section 7 shall be provided between the safety barrier and the vehicle parapet.
NOTE
The performance classes of the parapet and safety barrier can differ in containment level, normalised
working width class, normalised vehicle intrusion class, and ISL.
3.60
Where a safety barrier is to connect into an existing parapet, the existing parapet, transitions and/or
connections shall be assessed in accordance with CS 461 [Ref 2.N] to determine whether the existing
parapet, transitions and/or connections require replacement.
3.61
The parapet shall be capable of resisting forces applied via or through the safety barrier or transition.
3.62
Where there is two-way traffic, both ends of the parapet shall be treated as leading ends.
3.63
The traffic face of the safety barrier and vehicle parapet shall have a flowing alignment, with any
changes in alignment in accordance with the manufacturer's product description and associated
installation instructions.
3.64
The minimum containment level of the safety barrier at each end of the parapet shall be N2.
3.65
The minimum length required for full containment shall not include any change in set-back diverging
from the adjacent road alignment, nor terminal.
3.65.1
The minimum length required for full containment may include the length of any transition between the
parapet and safety barrier.
42

3. Requirements for permanent safety barriers
3.66
For new installations, a vehicle parapet connected to safety barriers via transitions shall be preferred to
a safety barrier being continued across a bridge or a structure on its conventional alignment in front of a
vehicle parapet.
3.67
Where a safety barrier is continued across a bridge or a structure on its conventional alignment and in
front of a vehicle parapet which has the specified containment level, the safety barrier shall meet the
normalised working width class, normalised vehicle intrusion class (where applicable) and set-back
requirements.
3.68
Where a safety barrier is continued across a bridge or a structure on its conventional alignment and in
front of a vehicle parapet which does not have the specified containment level, the safety barrier shall
meet the containment level, normalised working width class, normalised vehicle intrusion class (where
applicable) and set-back requirements.
Safety barrier provision and walking, cycling and horse rider (WCH) users
3.69
The need to accommodate a right of way through a continuous safety barrier shall be minimised.
NOTE 1
The requirements for designing for walking, cycling and horse riding are contained within CD 143 [Ref
17.N] and include the gap width requirements for WCH users.
NOTE 2
The requirements for walking, cycling and horse-riding assessments and reviews are contained within
GG 142 [Ref 55.I].
3.70
Where accommodating a right of way through a continuous safety barrier, the need for a pedestrian
guardrail shall be subject to a risk assessment.
3.71
Where accommodating a right of way through a continuous safety barrier where a need for a pedestrian
guardrail has not been identified, the arrangements in Figures 3.71a and 3.71b shall be followed.

3.73.1

3.74
Where accommodating a right of way through a continuous safety barrier where a need for a
pedestrian guardrail has been identified, the risk of a vehicle impacting a flat end or sharp corner of a
pedestrian guardrail shall be assessed, and reduced to a level which is SFAIRP.
3.75
Rights of way shall be located as far from the rear of the safety barrier as possible.
Safety barrier provision at noses and junctions
3.76
At the back of a nose, VRS, street furniture and other hazards shall be kept to a minimum and placed
as far downstream from the back of the nose as possible (refer to Figure 3.76).

Figure 3.76 Hazards and safety barrier adjacent to a nose - typical exit slip/link road layout
50
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025
