9. Requirements for pedestrian restraint systems
9.53
Where a metal pedestrian restraint system is located on a structure which is not on a WCH route, the
design of the pedestrian restraint system shall include features to reduce the risk of drivers and
passengers of broken down or damaged vehicles, maintenance staff, emergency service personnel
getting underneath or through the pedestrian restraint system, subject to the findings of a risk
assessment.
NOTE
Features to reduce the risk of drivers and passengers of broken down or damaged vehicles,
maintenance staff, emergency service personnel slipping underneath the pedestrian restraint system
can be provided by the addition of mesh and/or a bottom rail.
9.54
Where a metal pedestrian restraint system is not located adjacent to a WCH route, the posts and rails
shall meet the Class 1 nominal live loads in BS 7818 [Ref 68.N], unless the structure is over or adjacent
to a railway.
9.55
Where the Class 1 nominal live loads for posts and rails are assessed by risk assessment to be
inadequate, a higher class of nominal live loads for posts and rails shall be used, depending on the
outcome of the risk assessment.
9.56
Where a metal pedestrian restraint system is not located adjacent to a WCH route and goes over or is
adjacent to a railway, the posts and rails shall meet the Class 3 nominal live loads in BS 7818 [Ref
68.N], unless this is deemed to be inadequate by a risk assessment.
9.57
Where the Class 3 nominal live loads for posts and rails are assessed by risk assessment to be
inadequate, Class 4 nominal live loads for posts and rails shall be used, depending on the outcome of
the risk assessment.
9.58
Where a metal pedestrian restraint system is not located adjacent to a WCH route, and bar, sheet or
mesh infill is to be provided, the bar, sheet or mesh infill shall meet the Class B nominal loads in BS
7818 [Ref 68.N], unless the structure is over or adjacent to a railway.
9.59
Where the Class B nominal loads for infill are assessed by risk assessment to be inadequate, a higher
class of nominal loads for the bar, sheet or mesh infill shall be used, depending on the outcome of the
risk assessment.
9.60
Where a metal pedestrian restraint system is not located adjacent to a WCH route and goes over or
adjacent to a railway, and bar, sheet or mesh infill is to be provided, the bar, sheet or mesh infill shall
meet the Class D nominal loads in BS 7818 [Ref 68.N], unless this is assessed to be excessive by a
risk assessment.
9.61
Where a pedestrian guardrail is not located adjacent to a WCH route and goes over or adjacent to a
railway, bar, sheet or mesh infill is to be provided and Class D nominal loads are assessed as
excessive by risk assessment, Class B nominal loads shall be used.
9.62
Where a pedestrian parapet is not located adjacent to a WCH route and goes over or adjacent to a
railway, bar, sheet or mesh infill is to be provided and Class D nominal loads are assessed as
excessive by risk assessment, Class C nominal loads shall be used.
9.63
The minimum height of a new pedestrian guardrail not adjacent to a WCH route shall be 1000 mm,
unless the supporting structure goes over or is adjacent to a railway.
9.64
The minimum height of a new pedestrian parapet not adjacent to a WCH route shall be 1150 mm,
unless the supporting structure goes over or is adjacent to a railway.
9.65
The minimum height of a new pedestrian parapet not adjacent to a WCH route, but which goes over or
adjacent to a railway, shall be 1500 mm.
9.66
Pedestrian parapets of solid construction shall be designed to resist the more severe of the nominal live
load in Table 9.66 applied transversely at the level of the top of the pedestrian parapet, or wind loading
in accordance with BS EN 1991-1-4 [Ref 23.N] and NA to BS EN 1991-1-4 [Ref 89.N].

9.66.1
With reference to BS 7818 [Ref 68.N] and BS EN 1992-2 [Ref 24.N], a partial load factor of 1.5 should

Pedestrian restraint systems located adjacent to maintenance footpaths
9.67
Pedestrian restraint systems shall be provided adjacent to maintenance footpaths at the top of
embankments with
1)
a height of 0.5m or greater; and
2)
a gradient steeper than 1 in 2.
9.68
Pedestrian restraint systems adjacent to maintenance footpaths shall meet the Class 1 nominal live
loads in BS 7818 [Ref 68.N], unless this is deemed to be inadequate by a risk assessment.
9.69
Where the Class 1 nominal live loads for posts and rails are assessed by risk assessment to be
inadequate, a higher class of nominal live loads for posts and rails shall be used, depending on the
outcome of the risk assessment.
Suicide prevention measures and pedestrian parapets
9.70
Where the need for additional suicide prevention measures has been identified in the scheme suicide
review report, undertaken in accordance with GG 920 [Ref 72.N], suicide risk mitigation measures shall
be included as a design output.
9.70.1
At locations where vehicle containment is not required, suicide risk mitigation measures may include:
1)
the installation of a pedestrian parapet which has a minimum height of 2.3m and anti-climb
features; or
2)
increasing the height of an existing pedestrian parapet to a minimum height of 2.3m and installing
anti-climb features.
9.70.2
Anti-climb features should include a lack of toe or footholds.
NOTE
A foothold of 20 mm or greater is considered non-compliant with regard to 'anti-climb' conditions.
9.70.3
Anti-climb features may include the application of mesh to the front face of the pedestrian parapet and
or an inwardly sloping face.
9.70.4
Any pedestrian parapet installed as a suicide risk mitigation measure should be easier to scale from the
outside face in case an individual wishes to climb back to safety.
9.71
Where either a pedestrian parapet is proposed as a suicide risk mitigation measure, wind loading
calculations shall be carried out in accordance with CS 454 [Ref 3.N], on a site-specific basis.

10. Requirements for removable barrier sections
10.
Requirements for removable barrier sections
Impact performance requirements for removable barrier sections
10.1
For the purposes of testing to DD ENV 1317-4 2002 [Ref 45.N], the length of a removable barrier
section (RBS) shall be taken as the total length of the system (including any end posts), but does not
include any transition between the end posts and the connected safety barriers.
10.2
Connections to other VRS shall be made using transitions compliant with Section 7.
Use of RBS
10.3
The general requirements for an emergency crossing point/maintenance crossing point (ECP/MCP) in
Section 3 shall apply to RBS.
NOTE
Appendix D provides additional guidance on the use of RBS at ECP/MCPs.
10.4
The length of the leaves of the RBS (and hence, the overall length of the crossing) shall be identified
and given as a design output so that it is ensured that, when in the open position, the requisite number
of lanes are protected by the safety barrier.
10.4.1
The means by which the leaves of the RBS are opened/closed and the effect that this operation will
have on the practicability of opening and closing the RBS, should be identified.
10.5
The operation of the RBS shall not require specialist tools.
10.6
The method of opening the RBS shall be included as a design output, and will depend on the geometry
of the road where the RBS is to be installed, and the direction in which traffic will pass through the
opened section.
NOTE
Factors such as the gradient of the carriageway, location of other services and ground conditions are
important factors to consider when specifying the method of opening the RBS.
10.7
The layout shall consider, where applicable due to the type of RBS included in the design, the storage
location and storage requirements for any completely removable or demountable part(s) during
operational periods.
10.7.1
Depending on the design of the RBS, this may need to include consideration of wheeled sections.
Minimum containment level of RBS
10.8
The containment level of RBS shall be equal to or greater than that of the adjacent safety barriers.
10.9
The containment level of RBS shall be given as a design output.
Impact severity level (ISL) of RBS
10.10
The ISL for RBS shall be either ISL A or ISL B.
10.10.1
ISL A should be selected over ISL B as it affords a lower level of injury risk to the occupants of an
errant vehicle than ISL B.
Normalised working width class of RBS
10.11
The normalised working width class of the RBS when it is in the closed configuration shall not encroach
into the opposing carriageway.
10.12
The normalised working width class of the RBS when it is in the closed configuration shall be given as a
design output.
83

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
11.
Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint systems
General requirements for temporary vehicle restraint systems (VRS)
11.1
All installations that include temporary VRS shall be compatible throughout the entire installation length
(including safety barriers, terminals, transitions and crash cushions).
11.1.1
A temporary VRS may be either a permanent VRS erected temporarily, or a purpose made temporary
VRS.
11.2
The "Temporary Works" tab of the RRRAP [Ref 81.N] (tRRRAP) shall be completed as a part of the
temporary VRS design.
11.3
The temporary VRS provision shall be assessed in the temporary situation taking account of any
temporary traffic flow direction and location, and mandatory temporary speed limit.
11.4
All temporary VRS activities must be compliant with the UKSI 1999/3242 [Ref 80.N].
11.5
Permanent and temporary hazards having the potential to cause injury to the occupants of a vehicle,
the workforce or to other parties shall be identified within a site specific risk assessment, as required by
TSM Chapter 8 [Ref 85.N].
11.5.1
Common design factors that should be evaluated when using temporary VRS are listed below:
1)
length of time for which a temporary VRS is to be deployed;
2)
speed limits both existing and proposed;
3)
traffic flows including the percentage of heavy goods vehicles (HGVs);
4)
output of a CS 453 [Ref 75.N]assessment for any structures present in the work zone;
5)
containment level and working width class for normal containment safety barriers;
6)
containment level, working width class and vehicle intrusion class for higher and very high
containment safety barriers;
7)
length of safety barrier;
8)
road alignments, cross-section-pinch points, set-back, headroom clearance, available working
widths, etc;
9)
sight lines;
10)
drainage of carriageway;
11)
ground support and anchorage requirements for temporary VRS;
12)
clearances for operation and construction;
13)
requirements for temporary signing, lighting and road markings;
14)
obstructions that have the potential to affect the performance of the VRS, e.g. lighting columns,
signposts, etc;
15)
other VRS in the vicinity of the works;
16)
access arrangements for works vehicles, emergency and recovery vehicles;
17)
end of safety barrier details to reduce the risk of an errant vehicle passing behind the end of the
safety barrier and into the work zone; the reduced risk of the leading end of a temporary safety
barrier being impacted by a vehicle if flared away from the running lane, but the reduced space
available for site vehicles;
18)
the risk of an errant vehicle stopping abruptly in the running lane if a crash cushion is installed;
19)
the risk of a ramped leading end being installed causing a vehicle to end up in either the running
lane or the works area in the event of an impact;
20)
traffic movements approaching the temporary VRS;
21)
where applicable, the effect of the temporary VRS on the movement of road workers, WCH, etc;
22)
maintenance requirements;
84

11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
23)
traffic management and working space and widths required for installation and removal of
temporary VRS;
24)
compliance with contract specific specification.
NOTE
This list is not exhaustive and other factors potentially need to be assessed.
11.6
The site specific risk assessment required by TSM Chapter 8 [Ref 85.N], shall document the benefits
and dis-benefits of the options available to reduce the level of risk to SFAIRP.
11.7
The factors evaluated and decisions made shall be recorded in the design strategy record (or
equivalent).
11.8
Where the conclusion from the site specific risk assessment identifies that temporary VRS is needed to
reduce the level of risk to an acceptable level, or SFAIRP, temporary VRS shall be installed.
11.9
Factors that limit the choice of VRS for a particular situation shall be included as design outputs.
11.10
The full overtaking sight distance and stopping sight distance requirements of CD 109 [Ref 35.N] shall
be taken into account when positioning the temporary VRS.
11.11
The visibility requirements of CD 123 [Ref 29.N] shall be taken into account when positioning the
temporary VRS.
11.12
Any temporary signing, lighting and/or road marking requirements from CC 130 [Ref 7.N] which are to
be applied and/or attached to the temporary VRS shall be given as design outputs.
11.13
Where it is proposed to attach any temporary signing, lighting and/or road marking requirements from
CC 130 [Ref 7.N] to a temporary VRS, it shall be checked that the attachment of the temporary signing,
lighting and/or road marking does not adversely affect the dynamic impact performance of the
temporary VRS.
11.14
Any temporary signing, lighting and/or road marking shall not be applied and/or attached to the
temporary VRS unless permitted by the manufacturer's product description and associated installation
instructions for the temporary VRS.
11.15
Any specific access arrangements for works vehicles, emergency and recovery vehicles which
influences the choice of temporary VRS shall be given as a design output.
Set-back for temporary VRS
11.16
The minimum set-back for temporary VRS shall be in accordance with Table 11.16.
Table 11.16 Minimum temporary VRS set-back requirements (in mm)

11.17
Set-back shall be measured from the traffic side of the road markings/delineation markers (such as
temporary studs).
11.18
Justification for the use of relaxed set-backs shall be recorded in the Temporary Works tab of the
RRRAP [Ref 81.N] (tRRRAP).
11.18.1
Where space is available, an increase in lane widths or an increase in the number of lanes should be
included as a design output as the risk to drivers has the potential to be minimised where lane widths
are greater than the minimum in TSM Chapter 8 [Ref 85.N].

11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
11.19
Where a desirable or relaxed set-back is to be used with a speed limit exceeding 50 mph, set-back
shall be measured to exclude any longitudinal continuous trafficable profiles of a temporary safety
barrier, where it is agreed by the manufacturer of the temporary VRS.
11.20
Where a desirable set-back is to be used with a speed limit of 50 mph, set-back shall be measured to
exclude any longitudinal continuous trafficable profiles of a temporary safety barrier, where it is agreed
by the manufacturer of the temporary VRS.
11.21
Where a relaxed set-back is to be used with a speed limit of 50 mph, set-back shall be measured to
include any longitudinal continuous trafficable profiles of a temporary safety barrier.
11.22
Temporary VRS performance in restricted situations is unpredictable and siting of the temporary VRS
shall allow for deflection into the lateral clearance safety zone or into the adjacent running lane(s)
depending upon demonstrated system performance.
Temporary removal of permanent VRS to enable temporary works
11.23
The temporary removal of permanent VRS to enable temporary works shall not compromise the
performance of the remaining length of permanent VRS and its ability to protect hazards that are not
protected by the temporary provision.
11.24
Where temporary VRS is provided, the full performance of the permanent VRS shall be maintained to
the point at which the temporary system has achieved full height performance.
11.25
Where a temporary vehicle restraint system and a permanent vehicle restraint system are being
connected together, the requirements of Section 7 shall apply.
Requirements for temporary safety barriers
Minimum containment levels for temporary safety barriers
11.26
On roads where a speed limit of 50 mph or more is in operation, the minimum containment level for
temporary safety barriers shall be N2 unless a site specific risk assessment identifies that a minimum
containment level greater than N2 is required.
11.27
On roads where a speed limit of less than 50 mph is in operation, the minimum containment level for
temporary safety barriers shall be N1 unless a site specific risk assessment identifies that a minimum
containment level greater than N1 is required.
11.28
A site specific risk assessment shall include the output from the temporary works tab of the RRRAP
[Ref 81.N] (tRRRAP).
NOTE
A site specific risk assessment can include, for example, the output from a CS 453 [Ref 75.N] or a CS
461 [Ref 2.N] risk assessment.
11.29
Where a site specific risk assessment identifies that a containment level greater than the minimum level
is required, the greater containment level shall be included as a design output.
11.30
Where a site specific risk assessment identifies that a very high containment level is required, an H4a
containment temporary safety barrier shall be included as a design output.
11.31
Where the need for a higher containment level or very high containment level temporary safety barrier
has been identified, the nature of the risk, any mitigation with the steps taken to reduce the risk and the
resulting containment level required shall be recorded in the design strategy record (or equivalent).
11.31.1
Deep and/or wide temporary excavations may require a higher or very high containment temporary
safety barrier to be provided unless the output from the temporary works tab of the RRRAP [Ref 81.N]
demonstrates that a lower level of provision results in a level of risk which is SFAIRP.
Minimum containment levels for temporary barriers at permanent and temporary structural
supports
11.32
All permanent and temporary structural supports, such as bridge piers, temporary props and sheet
piles for temporary excavations shall be assessed in accordance with CS 453 [Ref 75.N] (existing
structures) or designed in accordance with BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] (new structures).

11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
NOTE
Abutments are not normally considered to be at risk as a result of vehicle collision as they are assumed
to have sufficient mass to withstand the collision loads for global purposes; (refer to CS 453 [Ref 75.N]
for existing structural supports, or BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] for new structures).
11.33
The minimum containment level of a temporary safety barrier in front of a structural support shall be N2.
NOTE
Where a normal containment level temporary safety barrier is required, the temporary safety barrier is
generally not intended to provide protection for the structure, only to reduce the risk of injury to road
users.
11.34
Where a CS 453 [Ref 75.N] assessment has been used to assess an existing permanent or temporary
structural support and a temporary safety barrier is to be provided in front of the structural support, the
minimum containment level of the temporary safety barrier shall be in accordance with Table 11.34, and
given as a design output.
Table 11.34 Minimum containment level of temporary safety barrier in front of existing
temporary or permanent structural supports
Categorisation of existing permanent or temporary structural
support according to CS 453 [Ref 75.N]
Minimum containment level
Group 1a or 1b (particularly at risk)
H4a
Group 2 (at risk)
H1
11.35
Where BS EN 1991-1-7 [Ref 19.N] has been used to design a new permanent and temporary structural
support, the minimum containment of the temporary safety barrier in front of the structural support shall
be in accordance with PD 6688-1-7 2014 [Ref 55.N], and given as a design output.
11.36
Where it is not possible to install a temporary safety barrier in front of permanent or temporary
structural supports to meet the full normalised working width and, for higher and very high containment
temporary safety barriers, normalised vehicle intrusion requirements, the temporary support shall:
1)
be designed to cater for the residual collision load component; and
2)
be located at least 200 mm from the rear face of the safety barrier.
Impact severity level (ISL) for temporary safety barriers
11.37
The ISL for temporary safety barriers shall be either ISL A or ISL B.
11.37.1
ISL A should be selected over ISL B as it affords a lower level of injury risk to the occupants of an
errant vehicle than ISL B.
Normalised working width classes and normalised vehicle intrusion classes for temporary
safety barriers
11.38
For normal containment level temporary safety barriers, the maximum identified value of normalised
working width class that the local hazard(s) allow, shall be given as a design output.
11.38.1
Where a normal containment level temporary safety barrier is a design output, and the temporary
safety barrier is to be used in a location with an enforced speed limit which is less than that at which the
temporary safety barrier was tested, the normalised working width class for the normal containment
safety barrier may be adjusted in the manufacturer's product description and associated installation
instructions to account for the enforced reduced speed limit.
NOTE
N2 containment level temporary safety barrier testing is carried out at 110 kilometres per hour
(approximately 70 mph), and hence the impact energy of an errant vehicle (due to the speed limit of
approximately 80 kilometres per hour) is significantly lower than that under which the temporary safety
barrier was tested.
11.39
No adjustment shall be made to the normalised working width class for higher or very high containment
temporary safety barriers.
87

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
11.40
No adjustment shall be made to the normalised vehicle intrusion class for higher or very high
containment temporary safety barriers.
11.41
The adjusted normalised working width of a temporary safety barrier shall not be less than the width of
the temporary safety barrier.
11.42
The use of an adjusted normalised working width for a temporary safety barrier shall be recorded and
justified within the temporary works tab of the RRRAP [Ref 81.N](tRRRAP).
11.43
The use of an adjusted normalised working width for a temporary safety barrier shall take account of:
1)
Any general site hazards and the interaction with the system during deflection;
2)
Any high risk site hazards which would lead to catastrophic events; and
3)
Secondary events due to an impact with a site hazard.
NOTE
Examples of high risk site hazards which would lead to catastrophic events include the collapse of an
excavation due to ground loading failure, or an impact with scaffolding.
11.44
The use of an adjusted normalised working width for a temporary safety barrier shall not result in
changes to the performance of the temporary safety barrier due to site conditions.
11.45
For higher and very high containment level temporary safety barriers, the maximum identified values of
normalised working width class and normalised vehicle intrusion class that the local hazard(s) allow,
shall be included as a design output.
11.46
For higher and very high containment temporary safety barrier systems, the required clearance to any
hazard that is vulnerable to residual loading shall be included as a design output.
Length of need of temporary safety barriers
11.47
Where it is possible for traffic to temporarily travel in both directions along the same carriageway or
along the hardshoulder under temporary traffic management, a risk assessment shall be used to
identify the length of need in both directions.
11.47.1
Where the length of need for a temporary situation exceeds the length of need for normal conditions,
the extra length of need may be provided only for the period of time that the temporary situation is in
operation.
11.48
A risk assessment shall be carried out to ensure the length of full containment safety barrier in advance
and beyond the hazard(s) or work zone(s) results in an SFAIRP level of risk with regard to a vehicle
reaching the hazard(s) and/or work zone(s).
11.49
A risk assessment shall be carried out at each proposed gap within a length of temporary safety barrier
to ensure that there is an SFAIRP level of residual risk.
11.49.1
The risk assessment should include the risks associated with;
1)
harmonic mean visibility, stopping sight distance and full overtaking sight distance;
2)
road user behaviour;
3)
location of the gap in relation to the road layout at that location;
4)
the purpose of the break in the barrier, and the type and entry speed of a vehicle using the gap;
and
5)
the location and aggressiveness of any exposed hazards.
NOTE 1
An envelope of exposure to a hazard(s) is created from the start to the finish of the gap in the
temporary safety barrier and accessibility needs to be considered from 90 at the leading edge of the
gap, to a projection of 20 from the trailing end of the gap into the work zone.
NOTE 2
A vehicle entering from the proposed gap can potentially be channelled further into the works than
normal due to both the verge/central reserve and the temporary safety barrier preventing a vehicle
exiting that restricted area.

11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
11.50
The location of any gaps in the temporary safety barrier shall be given as a design output.
11.51
Where two separate lengths of temporary safety barrier are provided, open or coned gaps in the
temporary safety barrier shall not be positioned such that an errant vehicle could pass through the open
or coned gaps in both lengths of temporary safety barrier.
Use of temporary safety barriers in contraflow operations
11.52
Where temporary safety barrier is used in contraflow operations, the following parameters shall apply:
1)
on each side of the temporary safety barrier, the dynamic deflection of the temporary barrier is to
be used as the minimum value of set-back if the numerical value of dynamic deflection exceeds
the set-back requirement given in Table 11.16;
2)
no gaps created in the temporary safety barrier between the two opposing flows of traffic in the
contraflow;
3)
an acceptable means of access provided for the emergency services and recovery vehicles to
attend to accidents or breakdowns within the contraflow at no less than 2km intervals, if identified
as a necessary measure.
NOTE
Acceptable means of access for the emergency services and recovery vehicles to attend can be found
within the section entitled General requirements for an emergency crossing point/maintenance crossing
point (ECP/MCP) in Section 3 of this document.
11.52.1
The trailing end of a safety barrier used in contraflow may be placed adjacent to, and overlap, a
permanent safety barrier after the return crossover has been achieved, subject to a risk assessment.
11.52.2
An energy absorbing terminal, may be used to start a length of temporary safety barrier installed within
contraflow, and the terminal could be installed adjacent to a running lane, subject to a risk assessment.
11.52.3
Temporary safety barrier used in contraflow may be tied into an existing permanent safety barrier at its
start or end, where it is connected using an permanent or temporary transition which is compliant with
Section 7.
Requirements for temporary terminals
General requirements for temporary terminals
11.53
Factors that limit the choice of temporary terminal for a particular situation shall be included as a design
output.
NOTE
A temporary terminal with a ramped design which is installed on the trailing end of a temporary safety
barrier can be difficult to see when reversing maintenance machinery. This can result in damage to the
temporary terminal. In such locations a different type of temporary terminal and/or additional measures
to mitigate the risk of an accidental impact is necessary.
Performance class of temporary terminals
11.54
On roads with a speed limit of 50 mph or more, temporary terminals that face oncoming traffic shall
have a performance class of either T110 or T110/1 as defined by PD CEN/TS 1317-7 [Ref 60.N], or P4
as defined by DD ENV 1317-4 2002 [Ref 45.N].
NOTE
On a two-way single carriageway road, the temporary terminals on both ends of a safety barrier are
examples of temporary terminals that face oncoming traffic.
11.55
On roads with a speed limit of 50 mph or more, temporary terminals that do not face oncoming traffic
shall have a minimum performance class of T80/3 as defined by PD CEN/TS 1317-7 [Ref 60.N], or P1
as defined by DD ENV 1317-4 2002 [Ref 45.N].
NOTE
Examples of temporary terminals that do not face oncoming traffic include temporary terminals on the
trailing ends of temporary safety barriers on dual carriageway roads, temporary terminals on the trailing
end of a temporary safety barrier on a one-way road, or leading end temporary terminals which are
located behind a sufficient length of temporary safety barrier.

11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
11.56
On roads with a speed limit of less than 50 mph, temporary terminals shall have a minimum
performance class of T80/3 as defined by PD CEN/TS 1317-7 [Ref 60.N], or P1 as defined by DD ENV
1317-4 2002 [Ref 45.N].
11.57
Temporary terminal performance class shall be included as a design output for both the leading and
trailing ends of each temporary safety barrier, including at the ends of gaps in temporary safety barrier
which are closed with cones, except for locations where the temporary safety barrier transitions into
another VRS.
Energy absorbing characteristic of temporary terminals
11.58
On roads with a speed limit of 50 mph or more, leading temporary terminals (that face oncoming traffic),
shall be energy absorbing.
NOTE 1
An example of temporary terminals that face oncoming traffic are temporary terminals on both ends of
a temporary safety barrier on a two-way single carriageway road.
NOTE 2
On roads with a speed limit of less than 50 mph, temporary terminals do not need to be energy
absorbing.
11.59
The energy absorbing characteristic of a temporary terminal shall be included as a design output for
both the leading and trailing ends of each temporary safety barrier, including at the ends of gaps in
temporary safety barrier which are closed with cones, except for locations where the temporary safety
barrier transitions into another VRS.
Single and double sided temporary terminals
11.60
Where a temporary terminal is located in a position where it is only possible for it to be struck on one
side, a single sided temporary terminal shall be included as a design output.
11.61
Where a temporary terminal is located in a position where it is possible for it to be struck on either side,
a double sided temporary terminal shall be included as a design output.
Direction of traffic past temporary terminals
11.62
The direction of traffic past temporary terminal value (one or two) shall be included as a design output.
11.63
A temporary terminal located such that it is possible for it to be struck in only one direction shall have a
direction of traffic past temporary terminal value of one.
11.63.1
A check should be made to ensure that the direction in which the temporary terminal has been tested is
the same as the one direction in which it could be struck when installed.
NOTE
Examples of a temporary terminal that can be struck in only one direction would be a temporary
terminal in the verge of a motorway, on a slip road, at a nosing between two carriageways travelling in
the same direction, or on a one-way road.
11.64
Temporary terminals located such that they could be struck in either direction shall have a direction of
traffic past temporary terminal value of two.
NOTE
An example of a temporary terminal that could be struck in either direction would be a temporary
terminal on a single carriageway road where there is no central reserve.
Impact severity level (ISL) of temporary terminals
11.65
The ISL for temporary terminals shall be either ISL A or ISL B.
11.65.1
ISL A should be selected over ISL B as it affords a lower level of injury risk to the occupants of an
errant vehicle than ISL B.
Lateral displacement zone class of temporary terminals
11.66
The maximum permissible lateral displacement zone classes for the temporary terminal shall be given
as design outputs to ensure that it is not possible for any part of a deformed temporary terminal to
90

11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
encroach into any hazard or area used by road users, road workers and/or other parties in the event of
an impact with the temporary terminal.
NOTE
PD CEN/TS 1317-7 [Ref 60.N] uses the term "permanent displacement zone" instead of "lateral
displacement zone".
11.67
Where a temporary safety barrier is to be flared to maintain set-back to the end temporary terminal, this
shall be included in the measurement of the available lateral displacement zone.
11.68
Where the temporary safety barrier is to be flared to maintain set-back to the end temporary terminal,
the space available for the flare of the temporary safety barrier and the set-back (to allow for the
temporary terminal) shall be given as a design output.
Exit box class of temporary terminals
11.69
For each installation, the maximum exit box class that the local hazard(s) allow, shall be given as a
design output.
NOTE
PD CEN/TS 1317-7 [Ref 60.N] uses the term "redirection zone" instead of "exit box class".
11.70
The exit box class for the temporary terminal (Z1, Z2, Z3 or Z4) shall be given as a design output to
ensure that it is not possible for any redirected impacting vehicle to encroach into any temporary or
permanent hazard or area used by road users, road workers and/or other parties, apart from being
redirected back onto the hard shoulder/carriageway which it had previously left.
11.70.1
Errant vehicles could end up on the departure side of temporary terminals with exit box classes Z3 and
Z4 and therefore systems with exit box classes Z3 and Z4 should only be used where there are no
hazards on the departure side of the temporary terminal which could be reached by an errant vehicle.
11.70.2
Other VRS may be located within the exit box class for the terminal, but not in advance of the terminal.
Locating two temporary terminals close to each other
11.71
Where two temporary terminals are to be located close to each other, the temporary terminals shall be
arranged such that they are outside of each others lateral displacement zone class and exit box class
and in such a way that SFAIRP, it is not possible for both terminals to be impacted simultaneously by
the same errant vehicle.
NOTE
Locating temporary terminals outside of each others lateral displacement zone class and exit box class
could mean that one temporary terminal is located further downstream than the other temporary
terminal.
11.72
Where two temporary terminals are to be located close to each other, the temporary terminals shall be
arranged such that the risk of a vehicle(s) travelling between the two temporary terminals is mitigated
SFAIRP.
NOTE
Where two terminals are to be located close to each other, a double sided temporary terminal is a
preferable solution where there is no difference in height between the two temporary terminals.
Requirements for temporary transitions
General requirements for temporary transitions
11.73
Temporary transitions shall provide a smooth change in alignment and not allow the safety barrier or
parapet to become exposed to a head on impact.
11.74
The vehicle restraint system(s) and/or structure on the leading and trailing ends of the temporary
transition shall be given as design outputs.
NOTE
Typically there is only one transition available between temporary VRS and hence the number of
design outputs required is limited.

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
Minimum containment levels for temporary transitions
11.75
The containment level of a temporary transition shall be the same as, or greater than, the lower
containment level of the two connected temporary VRS.
NOTE
For example if an N2 containment temporary safety barrier is connected to an H2 containment
temporary safety barrier, the containment level of the connecting temporary transition is to be N2 or
greater.
Requirements for temporary crash cushions
General requirements for temporary crash cushions
11.76
Temporary crash cushions shall be provided on the leading end of fixed and/or rigid objects (which may
be permanent or temporary) which are not to be removed, relocated or made passively safe, to reduce
the risk of injury to vehicle occupants in the event of an impact, where there is insufficient space to
install a compliant length of temporary safety barrier and temporary terminal.
11.76.1
Temporary crash cushions may be provided where:
1)
there is insufficient space to provide two single sided temporary terminals;
2)
there is insufficient space to provide a double sided temporary terminal; or
3)
it is not possible to install a temporary safety barrier and temporary terminal(s) in accordance with
the manufacturer's installation instructions.
NOTE
Fixed and/or rigid objects include roadside features such as bridge piers and toll booths where
protection is only required on a temporary basis.
Performance levels of temporary crash cushions
11.77
For roads with a speed limit greater than 50 mph, the performance level of the temporary crash cushion
shall be 110, as defined by BS EN 1317-3 [Ref 44.N], and given as a design output.
11.78
For roads with a speed limit of 50 mph or less, the minimum performance level of the temporary crash
cushion shall be 100, as defined by BS EN 1317-3 [Ref 44.N], and given as a design output.
Re-directive and non re-directive types of temporary crash cushion
11.79
Where a risk assessment has identified that the redirection of an errant vehicle by a temporary crash
cushion results in additional risk to the road user, road workers, or other parties, a non re-directive
temporary crash cushion shall be included as a design output.
11.80
Where a risk assessment has identified that a temporary crash cushion not redirecting an errant vehicle
results in additional risk to the road user, road workers, or other parties, a re-directive temporary crash
cushion shall be included as a design output.
Bi-directional and directional types of temporary crash cushion
11.81
The type of temporary crash cushion (bidirectional or directional) shall be included as a design output.
11.82
Temporary crash cushions located such that they could be struck from either direction shall be
bi-directional.
NOTE
An example of a temporary crash cushion that could be struck in either direction would be a temporary
crash cushion on a single carriageway road where there is no central reserve.
11.83
Temporary crash cushions located such that they could be struck from one direction only shall be
directional or bi-directional.
NOTE
Examples of a temporary crash cushion that can be struck in only one direction would be a temporary
crash cushion in the verge of a motorway, on a slip road, at a nosing between two carriageways
travelling in the same direction, or on a one-way road.

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
11. Requirements for temporary vehicle restraint ...
11.84
Temporary crash cushions shall be selected and positioned such that the direction in which the
temporary crash cushion has been tested aligns with the direction it could be struck, when installed.
Impact severity level (ISL) of temporary crash cushions
11.85
The ISL for temporary crash cushions shall be either ISL A or ISL B.
11.85.1
ISL A should be selected over ISL B as it affords a lower level of injury risk to the occupants of an
errant vehicle than ISL B.
Vehicle redirection zone class of temporary crash cushions
11.86
The vehicle redirection zone class for the temporary crash cushion (Z1, Z2, Z3 or Z4) shall be given as
a design output.
11.86.1
Errant vehicles could end up on the departure side of temporary crash cushions with exit box classes
Z3 and Z4 and therefore systems with exit box classes Z3 and Z4 should only be used where there are
no hazards on the departure side of the temporary crash cushion which could be reached by an errant
vehicle.
11.87
Where a re-directive temporary crash cushion has been given as a design output, the vehicle
redirection zone class shall be given to ensure that any redirected impacting vehicle does not encroach
into any hazard or area used by road users, road workers and/or other parties, apart from being
redirected back onto the carriageway which it had previously left.
11.87.1
Other VRS may be located within the vehicle redirection zone class for the temporary crash cushion,
but not in advance of the crash cushion.
Permanent lateral displacement zone class of temporary crash cushions
11.88
The permanent lateral displacement zone class for the temporary crash cushion (D1 to D8) shall be
given as a design output to ensure that no part of the deformed temporary crash cushion encroaches
into any hazard or area used by road users, road workers and/or other parties.
Use of temporary speed limits and temporary safety barriers
11.89
A temporary speed limit of less than 50 mph shall not be imposed on roads to protect short sections of
work that are to be carried out without closure or restriction of any of the running lanes, solely to allow
use of temporary safety barriers with a containment level of N1, or to allow the use of an adjusted
normalised working width class for temporary safety barriers with a containment level of N2.
93

12. Requirements for legacy systems
12.
Requirements for legacy systems
General requirements for legacy systems
12.1
Any proposal to retain an existing legacy RRS system shall be agreed with the Overseeing
Organisation.
12.1.1
Where replacement components are available on the market which are equivalent to those of the
legacy system, and which have been successfully impact tested, these should be preferred.
12.2
Where a new hazard is introduced in advance of, alongside or beyond an existing legacy VRS, the new
hazard shall:
1)
not be located within the normalised working width class of the legacy safety barrier;
2)
not be located, for higher and very high containment safety barriers, within the normalised vehicle
intrusion class of the legacy safety barrier;
3)
have a legacy safety barrier in front of it which has the correct containment level required in
accordance with the RRRAP [Ref 81.N]; and
4)
have the required length of need of legacy safety barrier in advance of, and beyond, the new
hazard.
12.3
Where VRS is being renewed at noses, merges and diverges, an assessment shall be made to
determine the cost/benefit of undertaking works on both VRS, retaining one or both of the legacy
systems, or replacing one or both of the legacy systems.
12.3.1
Works may be carried out on the main carriageway without fully upgrading the VRS on the slip road
even if the systems have, or are altered to have, a common end terminal.
12.3.2
Works may be carried out on the slip road without fully upgrading the VRS on the main carriageway
even if the systems have, or are altered to have, a common end terminal.
12.4
Where VRS is being renewed on the approach to, or departure from, a vehicle parapet or
vehicle/pedestrian parapet, an assessment shall be made to determine the cost/benefit of retaining the
legacy transition, or replacing it in accordance with CS 461 [Ref 2.N].
12.5
Where mitigation works are being carried out to legacy parapet transitions in accordance with CS 461
[Ref 2.N], an assessment shall be made to determine the cost/benefit of retaining the legacy system, or
replacing it.
12.6
Where it is proposed to upgrade an existing end terminal that includes a welded angle beam or a
terminal which does not meet the requirements of Section 6, an assessment shall be made to
determine the cost/benefit of retaining the attached legacy safety barrier system, or replacing it.
Additional requirements for connecting new VRS to an existing legacy system
12.7
Where a new VRS is being connected to an existing legacy system, the transition shall meet the
requirements for transitions in Section 7.
12.8
Where a new VRS is being connected to an existing legacy safety barrier the minimum length of legacy
system to be retained between anchorages, and the minimum number of adjuster or expansion beam
assemblies to be retained within each length of retained legacy system, shall meet the requirements in
Table 12.8.

13. Requirements for vehicle arrester beds
13.
Requirements for vehicle arrester beds
General requirements for vehicle arrester beds
13.1
Where the downhill longitudinal gradient of a carriageway is greater than the desirable maximum
longitudinal gradient in CD 109 [Ref 35.N], a risk assessment shall be undertaken in conjunction with
the relevant landowners, emergency services, local authorities and the Overseeing Organisation to
assess the need to provide an arrester bed.
NOTE
The function of a vehicle arrester bed is to decelerate a runaway vehicle on long, steep descending
gradients without causing significant damage to the vehicle, its occupants, other road users, adjacent
buildings or property.
13.1.1
Where the downhill longitudinal gradient of a carriageway meets or is less than the desirable maximum
longitudinal gradient in CD 109 [Ref 35.N] and where there is a history of runaway vehicle incidents, a
risk assessment should be undertaken in conjunction with the relevant landowners, emergency
services, local authorities and the Overseeing Organisation to assess the need to provide an arrester
bed.
NOTE 1
Whilst vehicle arrester beds are suitable for most types of vehicle, they are particularly effective in
bringing to rest large commercial vehicles which suffer brake or gear change mechanism failures.
NOTE 2
Generally, vehicle arrester beds comprise an area of ground excavated and replaced with a granular
material and having a paved approach.
13.2
The type of vehicle arrester bed, either 'remote' or 'adjacent', shall be given as a design output.
NOTE
There are two basic layouts for arrester beds; 'remote' which are incorporated into a separate escape
lane leading off the main carriageway, and 'adjacent' which are constructed adjacent to the nearside of
the carriageway in a widened section of the highway.
13.3
On 'remote' vehicle arrester beds the horizontal alignment of the bed shall diverge from that of the main
carriageway to minimise the potential for the nearside wheels of the vehicle being in the gravel bed and
the offside wheels being on an increasingly adverse cross slope, which could induce vehicle
overturning.
13.4
The vehicle arrester bed shall be separated from the main carriageway by at least 2.0m.
NOTE
Requirements for the signage to the entrance to vehicle arrester beds can be found in TSM Chapter 4
[Ref 84.N] and Traffic signs images and drawing [Ref 64.N].
Geometric requirements for vehicle arrester beds
13.5
The vehicle arrester bed shall be designed for the potential speed of an unbraked vehicle at the
moment of contact, not the speed limit of the road.
NOTE
The potential speed of an unbraked vehicle at the moment of contact is affected by the gradient of the
road prior to the arrester bed.
Length of vehicle arrester beds
13.6
The length of the vehicle arrester bed shall be given as a design output.
13.6.1
The length of the vehicle arrester bed should be sufficient to decelerate a large articulated vehicle with
multiple axle groups.
NOTE
A large articulated vehicle with multiple axle groups is likely to have the highest entry velocity and the
lowest average deceleration rate.
13.7
The minimum length for a vehicle arrester bed, shall be as given in Table 13.7.
96
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

mph (assuming an unbraked
vehicle)
Minimum length of vehicle
arrester bed (in m) with a fill
depth of 350mm
Minimum length of vehicle
arrester bed (in m) with a fill
depth of 450mm

NOTE
Table 13.7 assumes that the vehicle arrester bed is located on level ground.
13.8
Where the expected entry speed is between the values in Table 13.7, extrapolation shall be used to
determine the minimum length of the arrester bed.
13.9
Where the fill depth is between the values in Table 13.7, extrapolation shall be used to determine the
minimum length of the arrester bed.
13.10
Where the bed surface is aligned on a downward gradient, the length of the vehicle arrester bed shall
then be increased by 3% for each one degree of slope.
Width of vehicle arrester beds
13.11
The width of the vehicle arrester bed shall be given as a design output.
13.11.1
A minimum bed width of 4.0 m should be provided.
13.11.2
A 'single track' type of vehicle arrester bed only has approximately 50% of the stopping effect of a full
width bed, so requires an increased bed length and should only be proposed when there are no
suitable alternatives.
NOTE
At severely restricted sites, where it is not possible to construct a full width 'remote' or 'adjacent' vehicle
arrester bed, a reduced width 'single track type' 'adjacent' vehicle arrester bed, which just
accommodates the nearside wheels of a runaway vehicle, can be the only feasible option.
Depth of vehicle arrester beds
13.12
Vehicle arrester beds shall have depths between 350mm and 450mm, with the depth gradually
increasing over an initial length in order to provide for smooth vehicle entry.
Fill material for vehicle arrester beds
13.13
Clean rounded, uncrushed hard gravel or artificial lightweight aggregate shall be used as a fill material
for a vehicle arrester bed.
NOTE
A lightweight aggregate fill material (e.g. Lytag) has proven, in service, to be an effective fill material for
vehicle arrester beds.
13.14
A sieving size of 10mm shall be used for the vehicle arrester bed fill material.
13.15
The vehicle arrester bed material shall be free draining.
13.16
The base of the vehicle arrester bed shall be drained to avoid water ponding and potential freezing in
winter conditions.
13.17
Kerbing with a 300 mm upstand shall be installed at the side of a 'remote' vehicle arrester bed to assist
in restricting sideways movement and scatter of the fill material.
97

13. Requirements for vehicle arrester beds
13.18
On the 'adjacent' type of vehicle arrester bed any kerbing used for the edge restraint between the
gravel bed and the main carriageway pavement shall be level with the carriageway so that a vehicle
driver, who has the potential to overshoot the signed entry to the bed, might still steer into the vehicle
arrester bed.
NOTE
A flush edge on the carriageway side can also facilitate easier retrieval of errant vehicles.

14. Requirements for anti-glare systems mounted o...
14.
Requirements for anti-glare systems mounted on road restraint
systems
General requirements for anti-glare systems mounted on road restraint systems
14.1
The introduction of anti-glare screens shall be subject to a risk assessment.
NOTE 1
Anti-glare screens can be particularly effective alongside roads with a speed limit of 50 mph or greater
which have an adjacent minor road where, in darkness, opposing headlamps on the nearside could
cause confusion.
NOTE 2
At rural locations where swept headlamp paths persistently cross drivers' vision, short lengths of
anti-glare screens could improve safety.
NOTE 3
Anti-glare screens in central reserves do not appear to significantly alter injury accident rates.
NOTE 4
Anti-glare screening can be ineffective where there is severe undulation of the highway alignment, and
where the proportion of large goods vehicle traffic is high.
NOTE 5
At exposed locations, anti-glare screen can also act as a sun block to the carriageway creating
potential for frost or ice hazards.
NOTE 6
Where the highway alignment contains tight right hand curves, the stopping sight distance can also be
detrimentally obscured by anti-glare screens leading to greater incident risk.
14.2
Anti-glare systems shall be designed so that light directed towards the driver at oblique angles (12 to
20) is reduced whilst relatively open vision (around 70) is maintained in the sideways direction.
14.3
The minimum height of the anti-glare screen from ground level shall be 2.0 m.
14.4
The risks associated with attaching an anti-glare system to a new or existing VRS shall be assessed
prior to installation.
14.4.1
The risk assessment associated with attaching an anti-glare system to a new or existing VRS should
include the change in the performance of the VRS due to the attachment of the anti-glare system, and
the risks associated with the detachment of the anti-glare system in the event of an impact with the
VRS.
14.5
Where the inclusion of an anti-glare screen could restrict a police authority's view of the opposing
carriageway or adjacent road, the police authority shall be consulted prior to the inclusion of any
anti-glare system into a design.
14.6
Any objections / limitations raised by the police authority during consultation to the inclusion of an
anti-glare screen, shall be adhered to.
Light screening capability of anti-glare systems mounted on road restraint
systems
14.7
The ability of the anti-glare system to block out all the incident rays, or block out partially the incident
rays shall be given as a design output.
Light transmission factor (Cti) of anti-glare systems mounted on road restraint
systems
14.8
The maximum acceptable values of Cti shall be as follows, where i is the angle between the axis of the
system and the direction of the incident ray:
1)
for angles of incidence 12, Cti 0,05
2)
either Ct15 0,05, or Ct18 0,2 and Ct15 0,15.
Accessibility category of anti-glare systems mounted on road restraint systems
14.9
Where accessibility is required through the anti-glare system, it shall fall into one of the two following
categories:
99

Category 1: designed to permit, at any place, the passage of personnel carrying stretchers from
one side of the base to the other without the need to remove a section of the anti-glare system.
2)
Category 2: not designed to permit access at any place, but requiring a section of the anti-glare
system to be removed to permit access.
NOTE
Accessibility category 2 could be appropriate in areas, such as urban areas, where it is desirable to
prevent pedestrians climbing over the base. This category of anti-glare systems could permit sections
to be removed by emergency service personnel.
14.10
Where accessibility is required through the anti-glare system, the accessibility category shall be given
as a design output.
Lateral visibility of anti-glare systems mounted on road restraint systems
14.11
Group 1 shall be given as a design output when good lateral visibility through the occluding elements is
required.
NOTE
In accordance with BS EN 12676-1 [Ref 1.N], if a 1m long rectangle of the same height as the
occluding elements is located behind the anti-glare system, and the surface area occupied by the
anti-glare system does not exceed 20 % of the total surface area of the rectangle, the anti glare system
is defined as Group 1.
14.12
Group 2 shall be given as design output for any other anti-glare system.

15. Requirements for cattle grids
15.
Requirements for cattle grids
Design requirements for cattle grids
15.1
Cattle grids shall be designed in accordance with BS 4008 [Ref 67.N], CS 454 [Ref 3.N] and BS
5502-22 [Ref 5.N].
15.1.1
Cattle grids should only be provided where alternative measures, such as gates, have been
investigated and found to be unsuitable.
NOTE
ORR 2021 2021 [Ref 49.I] provides additional requirements and advice for the provision of cattle grids
at railway level crossings.
15.2
An alternative means of by-passing a cattle grid shall be provided for users that are unable to cross the
grid but are entitled by law to travel along the particular highway in question, such as walkers and horse
riders.
NOTE
BS 4008 [Ref 67.N] provides design criteria for by-pass facilities.
Siting of cattle grids
15.3
A check shall be made that cattle grids and any associated by-pass are to be located within highway
land, unless a legal agreement with the relevant land owner has been entered in to.
15.3.1
New cattle grids should be located within the highway boundary.
NOTE
The legal responsibility for animals straying or lying on or at the side of the highway lies with their
keepers; however, the Overseeing Organisation has a vested interest in ensuring animals cannot
access the motorway and trunk road network due to the safety implications this can have. Placing
cattle grids within the highway boundary therefore provides the Overseeing Organisation with a greater
level of control over this risk.
15.4
Consultation with adjacent landowners and local public rights of way officer(s) shall be undertaken
during the design process, and any agreed additional requirements or conditions included as part of the
design.
15.5
Cattle grids shall not be located within 18 metres of a junction, measured from the projected kerb line,
or edge of carriageway if kerbs are not present, of the adjoining road to the start of the grid.
15.5.1
Cattle grids should only be located where there is a minimum of 18 metres of straight road on either
side of the grid.
NOTE
Cattle grids can be a skid/slip hazard, which is a particular problem for cyclists and motorcyclists.
Providing a straight section on the approaches removes the need for road users to turn while traversing
the grid, which reduces the risk of skidding/slipping.
15.5.2
Cattle grids should only be sited where there is desirable minimum stopping sight distance available on
each approach to the grid in accordance with CD 109 [Ref 35.N] for the relevant design speed.
15.5.3
In situations where a cattle grid is to be placed closer to a major road junction than the desirable
minimum stopping sight distance, unobstructed visibility should be available between the edge of the
major road and the grid, measured from centre point to centre point in the approach lane.
NOTE
It is necessary for vehicles to cross cattle grids at relatively low speeds. It is therefore important that
adequate visibility is available on the approach to cattle grids to ensure that road users can react and
slow down safely prior to reaching them.
101
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

Appendix B. Guidance on the specification of vehicle rest...
Appendix B. Guidance on the specification of vehicle restraint systems on
the approach to and across structures on low speed and/or low
flow roads
The guidance given in Table B.1 is intended to assist in dealing with specific situations on the approach
to and across structures on low speed and/or low flow roads where the use of a fully compliant vehicle
parapet or vehicle safety barrier system may be impracticable or may itself present a similar or greater
hazard to the public. These situations, where the application of these requirements is not appropriate,
are particularly prevalent on local roads.

Figure C.1 Determining the minimum length of need of safety barrier at a hazard(s)

Appendix C. Examples of determining the minimum total len...
C1
Example 1: Hazard in the verge of a dual carriageway
A gantry base is located in the verge of a dual carriageway and has a length of 5.5 m.
The minimum length of full containment safety barrier in advance of the gantry base that produces an
"acceptable" level of risk through the RRRAP [Ref 81.N](length B to C) for that particular location is
20.0 m.
The minimum length of full containment safety barrier beyond the gantry base that produces an
"acceptable" level of risk through the RRRAP [Ref 81.N](length D to E) for that particular location is 0 m.
Therefore the minimum length of need is 20 m + 5.5 m + 0 m = 25.5 m (length B to E).
According to the safety barrier manufacturer, full containment for their specific system is achieved 12.5
m from the end of the terminal.
Therefore the minimum total length of safety barrier between terminals (length A to F) is 12.5 m + 25.5
m + 12.5 m = 50.5 m.
C2
Example 2: Hazard in the verge of a single carriageway road
A sign post is located in the verge of a single carriageway and has a diameter of 0.1 m.
It is not possible to replace the sign post with a passively safe sign post.
The minimum length of full containment safety barrier in advance of the sign post that produces an
"acceptable" level of risk through the RRRAP [Ref 81.N](length B to C) for that particular location is
16.0 m.
The minimum length of full containment safety barrier beyond the sign post that produces an
"acceptable" level of risk through the RRRAP [Ref 81.N](length D to E) for that particular location is
10.0 m.
Therefore the minimum length of need is 16.0 m + 0.1 m + 10.0 m = 26.1 m.
However this is a minimum length of need value, and in this example the designer increases the
minimum length of need to 45.0 m to reduce the level of risk to an "acceptable" level.
According to the safety barrier manufacturer, full containment for their specific system is achieved 17.4
m from the end of the terminal.
Therefore the minimum total length of safety barrier between terminals (length A to F) is 17.4 m + 45.0
m + 17.4 m = 79.8 m.
118
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Appendix D. Guidance on factors for ECPs and MCPs
Appendix D. Guidance on factors for ECPs and MCPs
D1
Emergency and maintenance crossing points
There are various options available to create an ECP/MCP. They may take the form of a removable
barrier section, a section of precast concrete barrier which can be moved to permit access, and/or a
post and rail safety barrier which can be temporarily taken down, and provision made for temporary
terminals. Each option varies in cost and ease of operation. Systems that are not suitable to be
removed should be avoided near ECPs and MCPs.
For ECPs, the main requirement is the speed with which the ECP can be opened and operational; this
depends on the option chosen. It also depends on whether specialist equipment or personnel are
required to operate or open the ECP. In most cases, a time of less than 30 minutes to open the
removable barrier section or dismantle a permanent safety barrier would be desirable, however the
maximum opening operational time is a function of risk that should consider, as a minimum:
1)
accessibility;
2)
operational timescales;
3)
operational risks, for example manual handling;
4)
safe storage of the removed/demounted barrier sections; and
5)
locational safety, i.e. the potential for runaway gates on hills.
For MCPs, speed may not be an issue as the opening can be planned. Where regular maintenance is
required, for example, near tunnels or on long structures, then it may be beneficial to provide
permanent MCPs at each end that can be opened and closed quickly. For other situations, there are
two options. Provide an MCP from the outset, or create an MCP only when required (i.e. take down or
break out the permanent system).
D1.1
Removable barrier sections at ECP/MCP
A removable barrier section is the easiest to open, generally requires no, or minimal, specialist
equipment and can be opened in less than 30 minutes. However, personnel do need to be trained to
ensure that they know how to operate the removable barrier section and secure it properly after use so
that it is correctly fixed to the permanent or temporary safety barrier and will function as a permanent
safety barrier, when not in operation.
D1.2
Demountable safety barrier at ECP/MCP
A demountable safety barrier may be used to form an ECP/MCP.
If this option is chosen for an ECP, specialist personnel and equipment may be required to dismantle or
remove the safety barrier and this can take some time and may exceed the normal 30 minute
recommendation required for operational reasons. For an MCP a demountable safety barrier is a viable
option.
Where a concrete H1 or H2 safety barrier is used, an MCP can be created by breaking out the concrete
and removing the debris. This operation might take some time depending on length and require
specialist equipment. A precast or in-situ concrete section can then be installed when the MCP is no
longer required.
119
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 Version 5.0.0
Appendix E. Amendments and additions to BS 6779
Appendix E. Amendments and additions to BS 6779
Research, computer modelling and full scale impact testing which has been completed since the
publication of BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N] and BS 6779-2 1991 [Ref 36.N] have demonstrated that
some of the requirements in those British standards are no longer accurate and require amendment. In
addition, many of the references made within the aforementioned British standards have now been
superseded, primarily through the publication of the Eurocodes, and are therefore out of date.
This Appendix addresses those errors and inaccuracies until such time that BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]
and BS 6779-2 1991 [Ref 36.N] are updated.
Some of the changes and updates are critical to the performance of parapets designed to those British
standards, and hence it is necessary to record the required changes.
E1
BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]
E1.1
Clause 2 of BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]
The following amendments and additions should be made to Clause 2 of BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]:
1)
clause 2.1: first paragraph, third line delete "71" and replace with "65".
E1.2
Clause 5 of BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]
The following amendments and additions should be made to Clause 5 of BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]:
1)
Table 1  Designation of vehicle parapets: Item a), delete the existing text under "Designation"
and "Clause" and insert "As prescribed in BS EN 1317-1 [Ref 62.N], and BS EN 1317-2 [Ref
61.N]".
E1.3

E1.8
Additional requirements
The following additional requirements should be made to BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]:
"Sealing of voids in anchorages, attachment systems and base plates with a non-structural
impermeable durable filler is important to prevent water ingress and to avoid corrosion and damage.
Fillers may be derived from polymers or elastomers, but shall exclude acrylic or polyester based
materials."
"Prior to the anticipated start of manufacture of vehicle parapet components copies of the most recent
certified destructive test reports covering those component types to be supplied under the Contract
should be examined."
"Arrangements should be made for sample components and/or joints for destructive testing to be
selected at the works and on site by a Welding Inspector certified by CSWIP or equivalent. Selection
should be made taking into account the manufacturer's inspection reports, previous destructive test
reports and observations of current production practice on similar component types. Samples should
be selected on the basis that they represent the lower end of quality in the production batch. Particular
attention should be given to any features which could adversely affect the true throat size or the
mechanical properties of the materials."
"For the purposes of defining component types in sub-clause 9.4.3.2.2 of BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N],
differences in either member cross-sectional shape, joint configuration or weld type, constitute a change
in component type. Variations in cross-section size or member length need not constitute a change in
component type. Variations in parent metal thickness or weld throat dimension from the specified sizes
on the sample selected for destructive test may be included within the same type up to a limit of  40%."
"Sample components and/or joints selected for destructive testing in accordance with sub-clause
9.4.3.2.2 of BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N] should be indelibly marked and dispatched to a testing
laboratory appropriately accredited in accordance with sub-Clauses 105.3 and 105.4 for weld testing
and in the case of aluminium alloy posts, for static load testing in accordance with Annex E of BS
6779-1 1998 [Ref 37.N]."

Appendix E. Amendments and additions to BS 6779
"The following points should be considered when ascertaining the acceptability of components subject
to destructive testing
1)
Aluminium alloy posts should be static load tested in accordance with BS 6779-1 1998 [Ref
37.N]clause E.1 and E.2 prior to destructive examination.
2)
When conducting destructive examination each length of weld between weld ends or changes of
direction should be sectioned at intervals not exceeding 100 mm. One side of each section
should be ground, filed, linished or machined to an appropriate finish so that the actual throat and
leg dimensions can be measured and any discontinuities exposed. One fracture test in
accordance with BS EN 1320 1997 [Ref 20.I] on a length of weld of not less than 50 mm should
be made for each joint type on each component. Additional sections and fracture tests may be
required in cases of borderline acceptance. Non-compliances with the imperfection acceptance
levels of BS EN ISO 15614-2 2005 [Ref 51.I] and BS EN ISO 15614-1 2019 [Ref 50.I], as
appropriate, should be recorded.
3)
One representative section from each joint type for each type of component should be prepared
for macro-examination. A hardness survey should be done where any of the parent material is 20
mm thick or greater. An additional macro-examination should be made of each non-conforming
weld.
4)
For steel posts designed to fail plastically under impact yield/proof stress should be the same or
within normal statistical variation of that of the tested prototype.
5)
For aluminium posts designed to fail in a frangible manner the ultimate strength of the alloy
should also be as the strength of the prototype.
6)
For both steel and aluminium the extension at break should also be comparable to the prototype.
7)
For rolled or extruded sections the test samples shall be free from laminations or poorly formed
fusions."
"The results of the destructive tests including macrographs and load deflection history, where
relevant,should be reported and a certified copy sent to the manufacturer. In the event of
non-conformances being found the Contractor and the manufacturer should be notified as soon as
possible. The test specimens, uniquely identified by hard stamped marks should be returned to the
manufacturer's works."

E2.12
Additional requirements
The following additional requirements should be made to BS 6779-2 1991 [Ref 36.N]:
"Reinforced concrete vehicle parapet panel walls shall have a minimum thickness of 180mm for normal
containment level (N2), and 325mm at the critical design section for very high containment level (H4a)."
"Reinforced concrete vehicle parapet panel walls shall have a minimum length of 2.0m and a maximum
length of 3.5m."
"N2 containment vehicle parapets in concrete construction are to be designed with shear transfer
provision between adjacent panels."
For N2 containment vehicle parapets in concrete construction, an equivalent static nominal load of
50kN is to be transferred between adjacent panels within the top 0.5m of the sections."
"The normal containment level of BS 6779-2 1991 [Ref 36.N]is considered to be equivalent to the
normal containment (N2) in BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 61.N], and the high containment level of BS 6779-2
1991 [Ref 36.N]is considered to be equivalent to the very high containment level (H4a) in BS EN
1317-2 [Ref 61.N]."

CD 377 - ENAA
England National Application Annex for
Requirements for road restraint systems
(formerly TD 19/06)
Version 1.0.0
Summary
This National Application Annex contains the National Highways specific requirements related to
road restraint systems.
Feedback and Enquiries
Users of this document are encouraged to raise any enquiries and/or provide feedback on the content and usage of
this document to the dedicated National Highways team. The online feedback form for all enquiries and feedback
can be accessed at: www.standardsforhighways.co.uk/feedback.
This is a controlled document.

Appendix E/A. Process for the appointment of independent reviewers (for letters of acceptance for non-
harmonised products)
20

Foreword
Publishing information
This document is published by National Highways.
This document supersedes all previous revisions of CD 377 [Ref 12.N] ENAA and CHE Memo 441,
which are withdrawn.
This document provides requirements and advice used to develop the design outputs required to
deliver the works in accordance with CC 400 [Ref 9.N], CC 401 [Ref 8.N] and CC 402 [Ref 19.N].
Contractual and legal considerations
This document forms part of the works specification. It does not purport to include all the necessary
provisions of a contract. Users are responsible for applying all appropriate documents applicable to
their contract.

CD 377 - ENAA Version 1.0.0
Introduction
Introduction
Background
This National Application Annex contains the National Highways-specific requirements related to road
restraint systems.
This document is an update to CD 377 [Ref 12.N] ENAA and reflects changes to National Highways'
requirements. It also takes account of updated and new EU standards.
This document gives requirements for road restraint systems and it, together with the associated
RRRAP [Ref 20.N] and RRRAP User Guide [Ref 3.N], assists those involved in determining where road
restraint systems are warranted, and the minimum required parameters.
Assumptions made in the preparation of this document
The assumptions made in GG 101 [Ref 4.N] apply to this document.
This document is written on the basis that road restraint systems are supplied and constructed in
accordance with the Manual of Contract Documents for Highway Works - CC 400 [Ref 9.N], CC 401
[Ref 8.N] and CC 402 [Ref 19.N], and all other works are designed and specified in accordance with the
Design Manual for Roads and Bridges (DMRB).
Mutual Recognition
Where there is a requirement in this document for compliance with any part of a British Standard,
technical specification or quality mark, that requirement may be met by compliance with the Mutual
Recognition clause in GG 101 [Ref 4.N].
5
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 377 Version 5.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

CD 377 - ENAA Version 1.0.0
Terms and definitions
Terms and definitions
Term
Definition
Edge protection
A fence or railing provided at the edge of a structure to
delineate the edge of the structure.
Letter of acceptance
A letter signed by one of the independent reviewers
appointed by National Highways (under the conditions
within Appendix E/A) which:
1) confirms that the specific non-harmonised product
which is to be incorporated into a design complies
with BS EN 1317-1 [Ref 17.N] and the relevant part
of BS EN 1317 for the product type (refer to CC
400 [Ref 9.N] and CC 402 [Ref 19.N]);
2) identifies any restrictions on the use of the product;
and
3) includes a copy of the independent reviewer's letter
of appointment, issued by National Highways.
Like-for-like
Replacing a specific component detailed in the
manufacturer's instruction or NPSBS v1 2005 [Ref
6.N]with the same specific component.
Changing beams for other permitted variants from the
system with the same performance, as permitted by the
manufacturer or as described in NPSBS v1 2005 [Ref
6.N] or Section 4 of BS 7669-3 [Ref 22.N].
Changing post types and location as permitted by the
manufacturer or as described in NPSBS v1 2005 [Ref
6.N] or Section 4 of BS 7669-3 [Ref 22.N].
Replacement of obsolete end terminals in accordance
with manufacturer's approved installation manual. 1
Non-rigid safety barrier
A safety barrier that when tested in accordance with BS
EN 1317-1 [Ref 17.N] and BS EN 1317-2 [Ref 16.N],
deflects from its pre-impact position (i.e. it has a
dynamic deflection greater than zero).
Safety critical intervention
Works to renew condition expired components on a
like-for-like basis where a scheme is not due to renew
the section within an appropriate timescale.
Satisfactory condition
An RRS in condition Grade 1, 2 or 3 as defined within
National Highways document SED 02 01-GN04 [Ref
21.N], is regarded as being in a satisfactory condition.
Safety, Engineering and Standards (SES)
VRS Adviser
A person employed by National Highways in the Safety,
Engineering and Standards (SES) VRS team.
7

title(s).
